<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Evolutionary history of plants</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">Evolutionary history of life</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trilete_spores.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="665" data-file-width="661" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Trilete_spores.png/220px-Trilete_spores.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Trilete_spores.png/330px-Trilete_spores.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Trilete_spores.png/440px-Trilete_spores.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trilete_spores.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Silurian" title="Late Silurian">Late Silurian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sporangium" title="Sporangium">sporangium</a>, artificially colored. <b>Green</b>: A spore tetrad. <b>Blue</b>: A spore bearing a trilete mark – the <i>Y</i>-shaped scar. The spores are about 30-35 μm across</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plant_phylogeny.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1481" data-file-width="940" height="473" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Plant_phylogeny.png/300px-Plant_phylogeny.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Plant_phylogeny.png/450px-Plant_phylogeny.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Plant_phylogeny.png/600px-Plant_phylogeny.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plant_phylogeny.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Phylogenetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a> tree, showing the major <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a> and traditional groups. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">Monophyletic</a> groups are in black and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletics</a> in blue. Diagram according to <a href="/wiki/Symbiogenesis" title="Symbiogenesis">symbiogenetic</a> origin of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_cells" title="Plant cells">plant cells</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and phylogeny of <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bryophyte" title="Bryophyte">bryophytes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plants</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>evolution of <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a></b> has resulted in widely varying levels of complexity, from the earliest <a href="/wiki/Algal_mat" title="Algal mat">algal mats</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Bryophyte" title="Bryophyte">bryophytes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycopod" title="Lycopod">lycopods</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fern" title="Fern">ferns</a>, to the complex <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">gymnosperms</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperm" title="Angiosperm">angiosperms</a> of today. While many of the groups which appeared earlier continue to thrive, as exemplified by algal dominance in marine environments, more recently derived groups have also displaced previously ecologically dominant ones, e.g. the ascendance of flowering plants over gymnosperms in terrestrial environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:498</sup></p>
<p>Evidence for the appearance of the first land plants occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a>, around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=450">450</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, in the form of fossil spores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wellman2003_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wellman2003-7">[7]</a></sup> Land plants began to diversify in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Silurian" title="Late Silurian">Late Silurian</a>, from around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=430">430</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, and the results of their diversification are displayed in remarkable detail in an early <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> fossil assemblage from the <a href="/wiki/Rhynie_chert" title="Rhynie chert">Rhynie chert</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Chert" title="Chert">chert</a>, formed in volcanic hot springs, preserved several species of early plants in cellular detail by petrification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the middle of the Devonian, many of the features recognised in plants today were present, including roots and leaves. Late Devonian free-sporing plants such as <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeopteris" title="Archaeopteris">Archaeopteris</a></i> had <a href="/wiki/Vascular_cambium" title="Vascular cambium">secondary vascular tissue</a> that produced <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a> and had formed forests of tall trees. Also by late Devonian, <i>Elkinsia</i>, an early <a href="/wiki/Pteridospermatophyta" title="Pteridospermatophyta">seed fern</a>, had evolved seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothwelletal989_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rothwelletal989-9">[9]</a></sup> Evolutionary innovation continued into the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> and is still ongoing today. Most plant groups were relatively unscathed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permo-Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permo-Triassic extinction event">Permo-Triassic extinction event</a>, although the structures of communities changed. This may have set the scene for the appearance of the flowering plants in the Triassic (~<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=200">200</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>), and their later diversification in the Cretaceous and <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a>. The latest major group of plants to evolve were the <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grasses</a>, which became important in the mid-Paleogene, from around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=40">40</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>. The grasses, as well as many other groups, evolved new mechanisms of metabolism to survive the low CO<sub>2</sub> and warm, dry conditions of the tropics over the last <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=10">10</a> million years</span>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonization_of_land">Colonization of land<span id="land"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Colonization of land">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="toccolours searchaux" id="Container" style="text-align: left; padding:0.5em 0 0 0; border-style:solid; float:right;clear:right; padding:0 0.5em; margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em; overflow: hidden;">

<div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;">

</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="toccolours" id="Legend" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div>
<div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#Two_dimensions" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">millions of years</a>.<br/>
<small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline">Human timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Nature_timeline" title="Template:Nature timeline">Nature timeline</a></i></small></div>
</div>
<p>Land plants evolved from a group of <a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">green algae</a>, perhaps as early as <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=510">510</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> some molecular estimates place their origin even earlier, as much as <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=630">630</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke2011_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke2011-11">[11]</a></sup> Their closest living relatives are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charophyte" title="Charophyte">charophytes</a>, specifically <a href="/wiki/Charales" title="Charales">Charales</a>; assuming that the Charales' habit has changed little since the divergence of lineages, this means that the land plants evolved from a branched, filamentous alga dwelling in shallow fresh water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KCbook_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-KCbook-12">[12]</a></sup> perhaps at the edge of seasonally desiccating pools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> The alga would have had a <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle#Haplontic_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">haplontic life cycle</a>: it would only very briefly have had paired <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a> (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyploidy#diploid" title="Polyploidy">diploid</a> condition) when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">egg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> first fused to form a <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygote</a>; this would have immediately divided by <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a> to produce cells with half the number of unpaired chromosomes (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyploidy#haploid" title="Polyploidy">haploid</a> condition). <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">Co-operative interactions</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a> may have helped early plants adapt to the stresses of the terrestrial realm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heckman2001_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heckman2001-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Devonianscene-green.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1144" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Devonianscene-green.jpg/200px-Devonianscene-green.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Devonianscene-green.jpg/300px-Devonianscene-green.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Devonianscene-green.jpg/400px-Devonianscene-green.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Devonianscene-green.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Devonian marks the beginning of extensive land colonization by plants, which – through their effects on erosion and sedimentation – brought about significant climatic change.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plant_Diversity_(2).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="383" data-file-width="512" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Plant_Diversity_%282%29.svg/200px-Plant_Diversity_%282%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Plant_Diversity_%282%29.svg/300px-Plant_Diversity_%282%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Plant_Diversity_%282%29.svg/400px-Plant_Diversity_%282%29.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plant_Diversity_(2).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">Cladogram</a> of plant evolution</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Plants were not the first photosynthesisers on land; weathering rates suggest that photosynthetic organisms were already living on the land <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1,200">1,200</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> and microbial fossils have been found in freshwater lake deposits from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1,000">1,000</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> but the <a href="/wiki/Isotopic_signature#Carbon_isotopes" title="Isotopic signature">carbon isotope record</a> suggests that they were too scarce to impact the atmospheric composition until around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=850">850</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knauth2009_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knauth2009-15">[15]</a></sup> These organisms, although phylogenetically diverse,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Battistuzzi2004_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Battistuzzi2004-16">[16]</a></sup> were probably small and simple, forming little more than an "algal scum".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first evidence of <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> on land comes from spores of mid-Ordovician age (early Llanvirn, ~<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=470">470</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gray1985_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gray1985-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wellman2000_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wellman2000-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubinstein2010_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rubinstein2010-19">[19]</a></sup> These spores, known as <a href="/wiki/Cryptospore" title="Cryptospore">cryptospores</a>, were produced either singly (monads), in pairs (dyads) or groups of four (tetrads), and their microstructure resembles that of modern <a href="/wiki/Marchantiophyta" title="Marchantiophyta">liverwort</a> spores, suggesting they share an equivalent grade of organisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wellman2003_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wellman2003-7">[7]</a></sup> Their walls contain sporopollenin – further evidence of an embryophytic affinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steemans2010_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steemans2010-20">[20]</a></sup> It could be that atmospheric 'poisoning' prevented eukaryotes from colonising the land prior to this,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kump2005_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kump2005-21">[21]</a></sup> or it could simply have taken a great time for the necessary complexity to evolve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butterfield2009_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butterfield2009-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Trilete spores similar to those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tracheophytes" title="Tracheophytes">vascular plants</a> appear soon afterwards, in Upper Ordovician rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steemans2009_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steemans2009-23">[23]</a></sup> Depending exactly when the tetrad splits, each of the four spores may bear a "trilete mark", a Y-shape, reflecting the points at which each cell squashed up against its neighbours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gray1985_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gray1985-17">[17]</a></sup> However, this requires that the spore walls be sturdy and resistant at an early stage. This resistance is closely associated with having a desiccation-resistant outer wall—a trait only of use when spores must survive out of water. Indeed, even those <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryophytes" title="Embryophytes">embryophytes</a> that have returned to the water lack a resistant wall, thus don't bear trilete marks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gray1985_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gray1985-17">[17]</a></sup> A close examination of algal spores shows that none have trilete spores, either because their walls are not resistant enough, or in those rare cases where it is, the spores disperse before they are squashed enough to develop the mark, or don't fit into a tetrahedral tetrad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gray1985_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gray1985-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest megafossils of land plants were <a href="/wiki/Thallus" title="Thallus">thalloid</a> organisms, which dwelt in fluvial wetlands and are found to have covered most of an early Silurian flood plain. They could only survive when the land was waterlogged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> There were also microbial mats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomescu2008_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tomescu2008-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Once plants had reached the land, there were two approaches to dealing with desiccation. The bryophytes avoid it or give in to it, restricting their ranges to moist settings, or drying out and putting their metabolism "on hold" until more water arrives. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tracheophytes" title="Tracheophytes">Tracheophytes</a> resist desiccation: They all bear a waterproof outer cuticle layer wherever they are exposed to air (as do some bryophytes), to reduce water loss, but—since a total covering would cut them off from CO<sub>2</sub> in the atmosphere—they rapidly evolved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomata" title="Stomata">stomata</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="this account implies that stomata first evolved in vascular plants, but they are present in the sporophytes of mosses and hornworts (July 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> small openings to allow, and control the rate of, gas exchange. Tracheophytes also developed vascular tissue to aid in the movement of water within the organisms (see <a href="#xylem">below</a>), and moved away from a gametophyte dominated life cycle (see <a href="#Changing_life_cycles">below</a>). Vascular tissue also facilitated upright growth without the support of water and paved the way for the evolution of larger plants on land.</p>
<p>The establishment of a land-based flora caused increased accumulation of oxygen in the atmosphere, as the plants produced oxygen as a waste product. When this concentration rose above 13%, wildfires became possible. This is first recorded in the early Silurian fossil record by charcoalified plant fossils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott2006_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott2006-26">[26]</a></sup> Apart from a controversial gap in the Late Devonian, charcoal is present ever since.</p>
<p>Charcoalification is an important <a href="/wiki/Taphonomy" title="Taphonomy">taphonomic</a> mode. Wildfire drives off the volatile compounds, leaving only a shell of pure carbon. This is not a viable food source for herbivores or detritovores, so is prone to preservation; it is also robust, so can withstand pressure and display exquisite, sometimes sub-cellular, detail.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_life_cycles">Evolution of life cycles<span id="phases"></span><span id="life_cycles"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Evolution of life cycles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Alternation_of_generations" title="Alternation of generations">Alternation of generations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Angiosperm_life_cycle_diagram-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="500" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Angiosperm_life_cycle_diagram-en.svg/220px-Angiosperm_life_cycle_diagram-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Angiosperm_life_cycle_diagram-en.svg/330px-Angiosperm_life_cycle_diagram-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Angiosperm_life_cycle_diagram-en.svg/440px-Angiosperm_life_cycle_diagram-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Angiosperm_life_cycle_diagram-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperm" title="Angiosperm">Angiosperm</a> life cycle</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All multicellular plants have a life cycle comprising two generations or phases. One is termed the <b><a href="/wiki/Gametophyte" title="Gametophyte">gametophyte</a></b>, has a single set of chromosomes (denoted <b>1N</b>), and produces gametes (sperm and eggs). The other is termed the <b><a href="/wiki/Sporophyte" title="Sporophyte">sporophyte</a></b>, has paired chromosomes (denoted <b>2N</b>), and produces spores. The gametophyte and sporophyte may appear identical – homomorphy – or may be very different – heteromorphy.</p>
<p>The pattern in plant evolution has been a shift from homomorphy to heteromorphy. The algal ancestors of land plants were almost certainly <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/haplobiontic" title="wikt:haplobiontic">haplobiontic</a>, being haploid for all their life cycles, with a unicellular zygote providing the 2N stage. All land plants (i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryophytes" title="Embryophytes">embryophytes</a>) are <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/diplobiontic" title="wikt:diplobiontic">diplobiontic</a> – that is, both the haploid and diploid stages are multicellular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup> Two trends are apparent: <a href="/wiki/Bryophyte" title="Bryophyte">bryophytes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Marchantiophyta" title="Marchantiophyta">liverworts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hornwort" title="Hornwort">hornworts</a>) have developed the gametophyte, with the sporophyte becoming almost entirely dependent on it; <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plants</a> have developed the sporophyte, with the gametophyte being particularly reduced in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seed_plant" title="Seed plant">seed plants</a>.</p>
<p>It has been proposed that the basis for the emergence of the diploid phase of the life cycle as the dominant phase, is that diploidy allows masking of the expression of deleterious mutations through <a href="/wiki/Complementation_(genetics)" title="Complementation (genetics)">genetic complementation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Thus if one of the parental genomes in the diploid cells contains <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a> leading to defects in one or more <a href="/wiki/Gene_product" title="Gene product">gene products</a>, these deficiencies could be compensated for by the other parental genome (which nevertheless may have its own defects in other genes). As the diploid phase was becoming predominant, the masking effect likely allowed <a href="/wiki/Genome_size" title="Genome size">genome size</a>, and hence information content, to increase without the constraint of having to improve accuracy of replication. The opportunity to increase information content at low cost is advantageous because it permits new adaptations to be encoded. This view has been challenged, with evidence showing that selection is no more effective in the haploid than in the diploid phases of the lifecycle of mosses and angiosperms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sz.C3.B6vRiccHockShaw13_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sz.C3.B6vRiccHockShaw13-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are two competing theories to explain the appearance of a diplobiontic lifecycle.</p>
<p>The <b>interpolation theory</b> (also known as the antithetic or intercalary theory)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyce2008_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boyce2008-30">[30]</a></sup> holds that the sporophyte phase was a fundamentally new invention, caused by the mitotic division of a freshly germinated zygote, continuing until meiosis produces spores. This theory implies that the first sporophytes bore a very different morphology to the gametophyte they depended on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyce2008_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boyce2008-30">[30]</a></sup> This seems to fit well with what is known of the bryophytes, in which a vegetative thalloid gametophyte is parasitised by simple sporophytes, which often comprise no more than a sporangium on a stalk. Increasing complexity of the ancestrally simple sporophyte, including the eventual acquisition of photosynthetic cells, would free it from its dependence on a gametophyte, as seen in some hornworts (<i><a href="/wiki/Anthoceros" title="Anthoceros">Anthoceros</a></i>), and eventually result in the sporophyte developing organs and vascular tissue, and becoming the dominant phase, as in the tracheophytes (vascular plants).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup> This theory may be supported by observations that smaller <i><a href="/wiki/Cooksonia" title="Cooksonia">Cooksonia</a></i> individuals must have been supported by a gametophyte generation. The observed appearance of larger axial sizes, with room for photosynthetic tissue and thus self-sustainability, provides a possible route for the development of a self-sufficient sporophyte phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyce2008_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Boyce2008-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The alternative hypothesis is termed the <b>transformation theory</b> (or homologous theory). This posits that the sporophyte appeared suddenly by a delay in the occurrence of meiosis after the zygote germinated. Since the same genetic material would be employed, the haploid and diploid phases would look the same. This explains the behaviour of some algae, which produce alternating phases of identical sporophytes and gametophytes. Subsequent adaption to the desiccating land environment, which makes sexual reproduction difficult, would result in the simplification of the sexually active gametophyte, and elaboration of the sporophyte phase to better disperse the waterproof spores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup> The tissue of sporophytes and gametophytes preserved in the Rhynie chert is of similar complexity, which is taken to support this hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyce2008_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Boyce2008-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kerpetal2004_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kerpetal2004-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tayloretal2005_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tayloretal2005-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_morphology">Evolution of morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Evolution of morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Xylem">Xylem<span id="xylem"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Xylem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Xylem" title="Xylem">Xylem</a></div>
<p>To photosynthesise, plants must absorb CO<sub>2</sub> from the atmosphere. However, this comes at a price: while stomata are open to allow CO<sub>2</sub> to enter, water can evaporate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Water is lost much faster than CO<sub>2</sub> is absorbed, so plants need to replace it, and have developed systems to transport water from the moist soil to the site of photosynthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Early plants transported water within the porous walls of their cells. Later, they evolved the ability to control water loss (and CO<sub>2</sub> acquisition) through the use of a waterproof <a href="/wiki/Plant_cuticle" title="Plant cuticle">cuticle</a> perforated by stomata that could open and close to regulate <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>. Specialised water transport tissues subsequently evolved, first in the form of hydroids, then tracheids and secondary xylem, followed by vessels in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flowering_plants" title="Flowering plants">flowering plants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The high CO<sub>2</sub> levels of Silurian-Devonian times, when plants were first colonising land, meant that the need for water was relatively low. As CO<sub>2</sub> was withdrawn from the atmosphere by plants, more water was lost in its capture, and more elegant transport mechanisms evolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> As water transport mechanisms, and waterproof cuticles, evolved, plants could survive without being continually covered by a film of water. This transition from <a href="/wiki/Poikilohydry" title="Poikilohydry">poikilohydry</a> to <a href="/wiki/Homoiohydry" title="Homoiohydry">homoiohydry</a> opened up new potential for colonisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Plants then needed a robust internal structure that contained long narrow channels for transporting water from the soil to all the different parts of the above-soil plant, especially to the parts where photosynthesis occurred.</p>
<p>During the Silurian, CO<sub>2</sub> was readily available, so little water needed to be expended to acquire it. By the end of the Carboniferous, when CO<sub>2</sub> levels had lowered to something approaching today's, around 17 times more water was lost per unit of CO<sub>2</sub> uptake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> However, even in these "easy" early days, water was at a premium, and had to be transported to parts of the plant from the wet soil to avoid desiccation. This early water transport took advantage of the <b>cohesion-tension</b> mechanism inherent in water. Water has a tendency to diffuse to areas that are drier, and this process is accelerated when water can be <a href="/wiki/Capillary_action" title="Capillary action">wicked</a> along a fabric with small spaces. In narrow columns of water, such as that within the plant cell walls or in tracheids, when molecules evaporate from one end, they pull the molecules behind them along the channels. Therefore, transpiration alone provided the driving force for water transport in early plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> However, without dedicated transport vessels, the cohesion-tension mechanism cannot transport water more than a few cm, limiting the size of the earliest plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-7"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> This process demands a steady supply of water from one end, to maintain the chains; to avoid exhausting it, plants developed a waterproof <a href="/wiki/Plant_cuticle" title="Plant cuticle">cuticle</a>. Early cuticle may not have had pores but did not cover the entire plant surface, so that gas exchange could continue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-8"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Banded_tube.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="805" height="89" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Banded_tube.jpg/220px-Banded_tube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Banded_tube.jpg/330px-Banded_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Banded_tube.jpg/440px-Banded_tube.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Banded_tube.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Banded_tube" title="Banded tube">banded tube</a> from the Late Silurian/Early Devonian. The bands are difficult to see on this specimen, as an opaque carbonaceous coating conceals much of the tube. Bands are just visible in places on the left half of the image. Scale bar: 20 μm</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To be free from the constraints of small size and constant moisture that the <a href="/wiki/Ground_tissue#Parenchyma" title="Ground tissue">parenchymatic</a> transport system inflicted, plants needed a more efficient water transport system. During the early Silurian, they developed specialized cells, which were <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignified</a> (or bore similar chemical compounds)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-9"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> this process coincided with cell death, allowing cell contents to be emptied and water to be passed through them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-10"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> These wider, dead, empty cells (xylem) were much more conductive than the inter-cell method, giving the potential for transport over longer distances, and higher CO<sub>2</sub> diffusion rates.</p>
<p>The earliest macrofossils to bear water-transport tubes are Silurian plants placed in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Cooksonia" title="Cooksonia">Cooksonia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards1992_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards1992-34">[34]</a></sup> The early Devonian pretracheophytes <i><a href="/wiki/Aglaophyton" title="Aglaophyton">Aglaophyton</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Horneophyton" title="Horneophyton">Horneophyton</a></i> have structures very similar to the <b><a href="/wiki/Hydroid_(botany)" title="Hydroid (botany)">hydroids</a></b> of modern mosses.</p>
<p>Plants continued to innovate new ways of reducing the resistance to flow within their cells, thereby increasing the efficiency of their water transport. Thickened bands on the walls of tubes are apparent from the early Silurian onwards<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niklas1983_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Niklas1983-35">[35]</a></sup> are adaptations to ease the flow of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niklas1985_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Niklas1985-36">[36]</a></sup> Banded tubes, as well as tubes with pitted ornamentation on their walls, were lignified<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niklas1980_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Niklas1980-37">[37]</a></sup> and, when they form single celled conduits, are referred to as <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tracheids" title="Tracheids">tracheids</a></b>. These, the "next generation" of transport cell design, have a more rigid structure than hydroids, preventing their collapse at higher levels of water tension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-11"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Tracheids may have a single evolutionary origin, possibly within the hornworts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qiu2006_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qiu2006-38">[38]</a></sup> uniting all tracheophytes (but they may have evolved more than once).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-12"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Water transport requires regulation, and dynamic control is provided by <a href="/wiki/Stoma" title="Stoma">stomata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:521</sup> By adjusting the amount of gas exchange, they can restrict the amount of water lost through transpiration. This is an important role where water supply is not constant, and indeed stomata appear to have evolved before tracheids, being present in the non-vascular hornworts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-13"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Endodermis" title="Endodermis">endodermis</a> probably evolved during the Silu-Devonian, but the first fossil evidence for such a structure is Carboniferous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-14"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> This structure in the roots covers the water transport tissue and regulates ion exchange (and prevents unwanted pathogens etc. from entering the water transport system). The endodermis can also provide an upwards pressure, forcing water out of the roots when transpiration is not enough of a driver.</p>
<p>Once plants had evolved this level of controlled water transport, they were truly homoiohydric, able to extract water from their environment through root-like organs rather than relying on a film of surface moisture, enabling them to grow to much greater size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-15"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> As a result of their independence from their surroundings, they lost their ability to survive desiccation – a costly trait to retain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-16"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the Devonian, maximum xylem diameter increased with time, with the minimum diameter remaining pretty constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niklas1985_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Niklas1985-36">[36]</a></sup> By the Middle Devonian, the tracheid diameter of some plant lineages<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> had plateaued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niklas1985_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Niklas1985-36">[36]</a></sup> Wider tracheids allow water to be transported faster, but the overall transport rate depends also on the overall cross-sectional area of the xylem bundle itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niklas1985_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Niklas1985-36">[36]</a></sup> The increase in vascular bundle thickness further seems to correlate with the width of plant axes, and plant height; it is also closely related to the appearance of leaves<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niklas1985_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Niklas1985-36">[36]</a></sup> and increased stomatal density, both of which would increase the demand for water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-17"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>While wider tracheids with robust walls make it possible to achieve higher water transport pressures, this increases the problem of cavitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-18"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Cavitation occurs when a bubble of air forms within a vessel, breaking the bonds between chains of water molecules and preventing them from pulling more water up with their cohesive tension. A tracheid, once cavitated, cannot have its embolism removed and return to service (except in a few advanced angiosperms<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (April 2008)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> that have developed a mechanism of doing so). Therefore, it is well worth plants' while to avoid cavitation occurring. For this reason, pits in tracheid walls have very small diameters, to prevent air entering and allowing bubbles to nucleate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-19"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Freeze-thaw cycles are a major cause of cavitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-20"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Damage to a tracheid's wall almost inevitably leads to air leaking in and cavitation, hence the importance of many tracheids working in parallel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-21"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ultimately, however, some cavitation incidents will occur, so plants have evolved a range of mechanisms to contain the damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-22"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Small pits link adjacent conduits to allow fluid to flow between them, but not air – although ironically these pits, which prevent the spread of embolisms, are also a major cause of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-23"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> These pitted surfaces further reduce the flow of water through the xylem by as much as 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-24"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Conifers, by the Jurassic, developed an ingenious improvement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pittermann2005_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pittermann2005-40">[40]</a></sup> using valve-like structures to isolate cavitated elements. These torus-margo<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> structures have a blob floating in the middle of a donut; when one side depressurises the blob is sucked into the torus and blocks further flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-25"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Other plants simply accept cavitation; for instance, oaks grow a ring of wide vessels at the start of each spring, none of which survive the winter frosts. Maples use root pressure each spring to force sap upwards from the roots, squeezing out any air bubbles.</p>
<p>Growing to height also employed another trait of tracheids – the support offered by their lignified walls. Defunct tracheids were retained to form a strong, woody stem, produced in most instances by a secondary xylem. However, in early plants, tracheids were too mechanically vulnerable, and retained a central position, with a layer of tough <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sclerenchyma" title="Sclerenchyma">sclerenchyma</a> on the outer rim of the stems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-26"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Even when tracheids do take a structural role, they are supported by sclerenchymatic tissue.</p>
<p>Tracheids end with walls, which impose a great deal of resistance on flow;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niklas1985_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-Niklas1985-36">[36]</a></sup> vessel members have perforated end walls, and are arranged in series to operate as if they were one continuous vessel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niklas1985_36-6"><a href="#cite_note-Niklas1985-36">[36]</a></sup> The function of end walls, which were the default state in the Devonian, was probably to avoid <a href="/wiki/Embolism" title="Embolism">embolisms</a>. An embolism is where an air bubble is created in a tracheid. This may happen as a result of freezing, or by gases dissolving out of solution. Once an embolism is formed, it usually cannot be removed (but see later); the affected cell cannot pull water up, and is rendered useless.</p>
<p>End walls excluded, the tracheids of prevascular plants were able to operate under the same hydraulic conductivity as those of the first vascular plant, <i>Cooksonia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niklas1985_36-7"><a href="#cite_note-Niklas1985-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The size of tracheids is limited as they comprise a single cell; this limits their length, which in turn limits their maximum useful diameter to 80 μm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-27"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Conductivity grows with the fourth power of diameter, so increased diameter has huge rewards; <b>vessel elements</b>, consisting of a number of cells, joined at their ends, overcame this limit and allowed larger tubes to form, reaching diameters of up to 500 μm, and lengths of up to 10 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-28"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vessels first evolved during the dry, low CO<sub>2</sub> periods of the Late Permian, in the horsetails, ferns and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selaginellales" title="Selaginellales">Selaginellales</a> independently, and later appeared in the mid Cretaceous in angiosperms and gnetophytes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-29"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Vessels allow the same cross-sectional area of wood to transport around a hundred times more water than tracheids!<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-30"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> This allowed plants to fill more of their stems with structural <a href="/wiki/Ground_tissue#Fibres" title="Ground tissue">fibres</a>, and also opened a new niche to vines, which could transport water without being as thick as the tree they grew on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-31"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup> Despite these advantages, tracheid-based wood is a lot lighter, thus cheaper to make, as vessels need to be much more reinforced to avoid cavitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperry2003_33-32"><a href="#cite_note-Sperry2003-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaves">Leaves<span id="leaves"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Leaves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_Isoetes_lacustris0.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2325" data-file-width="1385" height="369" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Illustration_Isoetes_lacustris0.jpg/220px-Illustration_Isoetes_lacustris0.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Illustration_Isoetes_lacustris0.jpg/330px-Illustration_Isoetes_lacustris0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Illustration_Isoetes_lacustris0.jpg/440px-Illustration_Isoetes_lacustris0.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
