The lycopod <i><a href="/wiki/Isoetes" title="Isoetes">Isoetes</a></i> bears <a href="/wiki/Microphyll" title="Microphyll">microphylls</a> (leaves with a single vascular trace).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vein_sceleton_hydrangea_ies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="800" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Vein_sceleton_hydrangea_ies.jpg/220px-Vein_sceleton_hydrangea_ies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Vein_sceleton_hydrangea_ies.jpg/330px-Vein_sceleton_hydrangea_ies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Vein_sceleton_hydrangea_ies.jpg/440px-Vein_sceleton_hydrangea_ies.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vein_sceleton_hydrangea_ies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The branching pattern of megaphyll veins may belie their origin as webbed, dichotomising branches.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leaf_1_web.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Leaf_1_web.jpg/250px-Leaf_1_web.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Leaf_1_web.jpg/375px-Leaf_1_web.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Leaf_1_web.jpg/500px-Leaf_1_web.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leaf_1_web.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Leaf lamina. The <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaf</a> architecture probably arose multiple times in the plant lineage</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leaves" title="Leaves">Leaves</a> today are, in almost all instances, an adaptation to increase the amount of sunlight that can be captured for <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. Leaves certainly evolved more than once, and probably originated as spiny outgrowths to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_defence_against_herbivory" title="Plant defence against herbivory">protect</a> early plants from herbivory.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Leaves are the primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" title="Photosynthetic">photosynthetic</a> organs of a plant. Based on their structure, they are classified into two types: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microphylls" title="Microphylls">microphylls</a>, which lack complex venation patterns, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megaphylls" title="Megaphylls">megaphylls</a>, which are large and have complex <a href="/wiki/Leaf#Venation_.28arrangement_of_the_veins.29" title="Leaf">venation</a>. It has been proposed that these structures arose independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Megaphylls, according to Walter Zimmerman's telome theory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zimmermann_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zimmermann-43">[43]</a></sup> have evolved from plants that showed a three-dimensional branching architecture, through three transformations—<b>overtopping</b>, which led to the lateral position typical of leaves, <b>plantation</b>, which involved formation of a planar architecture, <b>webbing</b> or <b>fusion</b>, which united the planar branches, thus leading to the formation of a proper <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaf</a> lamina. All three steps happened multiple times in the evolution of today's leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is widely believed that the telome theory is well supported by fossil evidence. However, Wolfgang Hagemann questioned it for morphological and ecological reasons and proposed an alternative theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Whereas according to the telome theory the most primitive land plants have a three-dimensional branching system of radially symmetrical axes (telomes), according to Hagemann's alternative the opposite is proposed: the most primitive land plants that gave rise to vascular plants were flat, thalloid, leaf-like, without axes, somewhat like a liverwort or fern prothallus. Axes such as stems and roots evolved later as new organs. Rolf Sattler proposed an overarching process-oriented view that leaves some limited room for both the telome theory and Hagemann's alternative and in addition takes into consideration the whole continuum between dorsiventral (flat) and radial (cylindrical) structures that can be found in fossil and living land plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> This view is supported by research in molecular genetics. Thus, James (2009)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> concluded that "it is now widely accepted that... radiality [characteristic of axes such as stems] and dorsiventrality [characteristic of leaves] are but extremes of a continuous spectrum. In fact, it is simply the timing of the KNOX gene expression!"</p>
<p>From the point of view of the telome theory, it has been proposed that before the evolution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leaves" title="Leaves">leaves</a>, plants had the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" title="Photosynthetic">photosynthetic</a> apparatus on the stems. Today's megaphyll leaves probably became commonplace some 360mya, about 40my after the simple leafless plants had colonized the land in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Devonian" title="Early Devonian">Early Devonian</a>. This spread has been linked to the fall in the atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> concentrations in the Late <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> era associated with a rise in density of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomata" title="Stomata">stomata</a> on leaf surface. This must have allowed for better <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a> rates and gas exchange. Large leaves with less stomata would have gotten heated up in the sun's heat, but an increased stomatal density allowed for a better-cooled leaf, thus making its spread feasible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beerling2001_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beerling2001-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rhyniophytes of the Rhynie chert consisted of nothing more than slender, unornamented axes. The early to middle Devonian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trimerophyte" title="Trimerophyte">trimerophytes</a> may be considered leafy. This group of vascular plants are recognisable by their masses of terminal sporangia, which adorn the ends of axes which may bifurcate or trifurcate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup> Some organisms, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Psilophyton" title="Psilophyton">Psilophyton</a></i>, bore <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/enation" title="wikt:enation">enations</a>. These are small, spiny outgrowths of the stem, lacking their own vascular supply.</p>
<p>Around the same time, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zosterophyllophytes" title="Zosterophyllophytes">zosterophyllophytes</a> were becoming important. This group is recognisable by their kidney-shaped sporangia, which grew on short lateral branches close to the main axes. They sometimes branched in a distinctive H-shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup> The majority of this group bore pronounced spines on their axes. However, none of these had a vascular trace, and the first evidence of vascularised enations occurs in the Rhynie genus <i><a href="/wiki/Asteroxylon" title="Asteroxylon">Asteroxylon</a></i>. The spines of <i>Asteroxylon</i> had a primitive vascular supply – at the very least, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Leaf_trace&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Leaf trace (page does not exist)">leaf traces</a> could be seen departing from the central protostele towards each individual "leaf". A fossil clubmoss known as <i><a href="/wiki/Baragwanathia" title="Baragwanathia">Baragwanathia</a></i> had already appeared in the fossil record about 20 million years earlier, in the Late Silurian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rickards2000_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rickards2000-52">[52]</a></sup> In this organism, these leaf traces continue into the leaf to form their mid-vein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan2001_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan2001-53">[53]</a></sup> One theory, the "enation theory", holds that the leaves developed by outgrowths of the protostele connecting with existing enations, but it is also possible that microphylls evolved by a branching axis forming "webbing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Asteroxylon</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2005_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2005-54">[54]</a></sup> and <i>Baragwanathia</i> are widely regarded as primitive lycopods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycopod" title="Lycopod">lycopods</a> are still <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/extant" title="wikt:extant">extant</a> today, familiar as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quillwort" title="Quillwort">quillwort</a> <i>Isoetes</i> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Club_moss" title="Club moss">club mosses</a>. Lycopods bear distinctive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microphylls" title="Microphylls">microphylls</a> – leaves with a single vascular trace. Microphylls could grow to some size – the <a href="/wiki/Lepidodendrales" title="Lepidodendrales">Lepidodendrales</a> boasted microphylls over a meter in length – but almost all just bear the one vascular bundle. An exception is the rare branching in some <i><a href="/wiki/Selaginella" title="Selaginella">Selaginella</a></i> species.</p>
<p>The more familiar leaves, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megaphylls" title="Megaphylls">megaphylls</a>, are thought to have separate origins – indeed, they appeared four times independently, in the ferns, horsetails, progymnosperms, and seed plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyce2002_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boyce2002-55">[55]</a></sup> They appear to have originated from <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dichotomising&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dichotomising (page does not exist)">dichotomising</a> branches, which first overlapped (or "overtopped") one another, and eventually developed "webbing" and evolved into gradually more leaf-like structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan2001_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan2001-53">[53]</a></sup> So megaphylls, by this "teleome theory", are composed of a group of webbed branches<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan2001_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan2001-53">[53]</a></sup> – hence the "leaf gap" left where the leaf's vascular bundle leaves that of the main branch resembles two axes splitting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan2001_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan2001-53">[53]</a></sup> In each of the four groups to evolve megaphylls, their leaves first evolved during the Late Devonian to Early Carboniferous, diversifying rapidly until the designs settled down in the mid Carboniferous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyce2002_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boyce2002-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cessation of further diversification can be attributed to developmental constraints,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyce2002_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Boyce2002-55">[55]</a></sup> but why did it take so long for leaves to evolve in the first place? Plants had been on the land for at least 50 million years before megaphylls became significant. However, small, rare mesophylls are known from the early Devonian genus <i><a href="/wiki/Eophyllophyton" title="Eophyllophyton">Eophyllophyton</a></i> – so development could not have been a barrier to their appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hao2003_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hao2003-56">[56]</a></sup> The best explanation so far incorporates observations that atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> was declining rapidly during this time – falling by around 90% during the Devonian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berner2001_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berner2001-57">[57]</a></sup> This corresponded with an increase in stomatal density by 100 times. Stomata allow water to evaporate from leaves, which causes them to curve. It appears that the low stomatal density in the early Devonian meant that evaporation was limited, and leaves would overheat if they grew to any size. The stomatal density could not increase, as the primitive steles and limited root systems would not be able to supply water quickly enough to match the rate of transpiration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beerling2001_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beerling2001-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Clearly, leaves are not always beneficial, as illustrated by the frequent occurrence of secondary loss of leaves, famously exemplified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cacti" title="Cacti">cacti</a> and the "whisk fern" <i><a href="/wiki/Psilotum" title="Psilotum">Psilotum</a></i>.</p>
<p>Secondary evolution can also disguise the true evolutionary origin of some leaves. Some genera of ferns display complex leaves which are attached to the pseudostele by an outgrowth of the vascular bundle, leaving no leaf gap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan2001_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan2001-53">[53]</a></sup> Further, horsetail (<i><a href="/wiki/Equisetum" title="Equisetum">Equisetum</a></i>) leaves bear only a single vein, and appear to be microphyllous; however, both the fossil record and molecular evidence indicate that their forebears bore leaves with complex venation, and the current state is a result of secondary simplification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor1993_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor1993-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">Deciduous</a> trees deal with another disadvantage to having leaves. The popular belief that plants shed their leaves when the days get too short is misguided; evergreens prospered in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_circle" title="Arctic circle">Arctic circle</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PETM" title="PETM">most recent</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_earth" title="Greenhouse earth">greenhouse earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shellito2006_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shellito2006-59">[59]</a></sup> The generally accepted reason for shedding leaves during winter is to cope with the weather – the force of wind and weight of snow are much more comfortably weathered without leaves to increase surface area. Seasonal leaf loss has evolved independently several times and is exhibited in the <a href="/wiki/Ginkgoales" title="Ginkgoales">ginkgoales</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">pinophyta</a> and certain angiosperms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerts1995_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aerts1995-60">[60]</a></sup> Leaf loss may also have arisen as a response to pressure from insects; it may have been less costly to lose leaves entirely during the winter or dry season than to continue investing resources in their repair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labandeira1994_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Labandeira1994-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Factors_influencing_leaf_architectures">Factors influencing leaf architectures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Factors influencing leaf architectures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Various physical and physiological forces like <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> intensity, <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_speeds" title="Wind speeds">wind speeds</a> etc. are thought to have influenced evolution of leaf shape and size. It is observed that high trees rarely have large leaves, owing to the obstruction they generate for winds. This obstruction can eventually lead to the tearing of leaves, if they are large. Similarly, trees that grow in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> regions have pointed leaves, presumably to prevent nucleation of ice onto the leaf surface and reduce water loss due to transpiration. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbivory" title="Herbivory">Herbivory</a>, not only by large mammals, but also small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insects" title="Insects">insects</a> has been implicated as a driving force in leaf evolution, an example being plants of the genus <i>Aciphylla</i>, that are commonly found in <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>. The now extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moas" title="Moas">Moas</a> fed upon these plants, and it is seen that the leaves have spines on their laminas, which probably functioned to discourage the moas from feeding on them. Other members of <i>Aciphylla</i>, which did not co-exist with the moas, do not have these spines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the genetic level, developmental studies have shown that repression of the KNOX genes is required for initiation of the <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaf</a> <a href="/wiki/Primordium" title="Primordium">primordium</a>. This is brought about by <i>ARP</i> genes, which encode <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_factors" title="Transcription factors">transcription factors</a>. Genes of this type have been found in many plants studied till now, and the mechanism i.e. repression of KNOX genes in leaf primordia, seems to be quite conserved. Interestingly, expression of KNOX genes in leaves produces complex leaves. It is speculated that the <i>ARP</i> function arose quite early in <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plant</a> evolution, because members of the primitive group <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycophytes" title="Lycophytes">Lycophytes</a> also have a functionally similar gene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Other players that have a conserved role in defining leaf primordia are the phytohormones <a href="/wiki/Auxin" title="Auxin">auxin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gibberelin" title="Gibberelin">gibberelin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cytokinin" title="Cytokinin">cytokinin</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leaf_diversity.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="941" data-file-width="699" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Leaf_diversity.jpg/250px-Leaf_diversity.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Leaf_diversity.jpg/375px-Leaf_diversity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Leaf_diversity.jpg/500px-Leaf_diversity.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leaf_diversity.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The diversity of leaves</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One interesting feature of a plant is its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phyllotaxy" title="Phyllotaxy">phyllotaxy</a>. The arrangement of leaves on the plant body is such that the plant can maximally harvest light under the given constraints, and hence, one might expect the trait to be genetically <a href="/wiki/Robustness_(evolution)" title="Robustness (evolution)">robust</a>. However, it may not be so. In <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, a mutation in only one gene called <i>ABPHYL</i> <i>(ABnormal PHYLlotaxy)</i> was enough to change the phyllotaxy of the leaves. It implies that sometimes, mutational adjustment of a single locus on the <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> is enough to generate diversity. The <i>abphyl</i> gene was later on shown to encode a <a href="/wiki/Cytokinin" title="Cytokinin">cytokinin</a> response regulator protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Once the leaf primordial cells are established from the SAM cells, the new <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system" title="Cartesian coordinate system">axes</a> for leaf growth are defined, one important (and more studied) among them being the abaxial-adaxial (lower-upper surface) axes. The genes involved in defining this, and the other axes seem to be more or less conserved among higher plants. Proteins of the <i>HD-ZIPIII</i> family have been implicated in defining the adaxial identity. These proteins deviate some cells in the leaf <a href="/wiki/Primordium" title="Primordium">primordium</a> from the default <a href="/wiki/Abaxial" title="Abaxial">abaxial</a> state, and make them <a href="/wiki/Adaxial" title="Adaxial">adaxial</a>. It is believed that, in early plants with leaves, the leaves just had one type of surface — the abaxial one. This is the underside of today's leaves. The definition of the adaxial identity occurred some 200 million years after the abaxial identity was established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> One can thus imagine the early leaves as an intermediate stage in evolution of today's leaves, having just arisen from spiny stem-like outgrowths of their leafless ancestors, covered with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomata" title="Stomata">stomata</a> all over, and not optimized as much for <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">light harvesting</a>.</p>
<p>How the wide variety of plant leaf morphology is generated is a subject of intense research. Some common themes have emerged. One of the most significant is the involvement of KNOX genes in generating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compound_leaves" title="Compound leaves">compound leaves</a>, as in the <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a> <i>(see above)</i>. But, this again is not universal. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">pea</a> uses a different mechanism for doing the same thing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Mutations in genes affecting leaf <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">curvature</a> can also change leaf form, by changing the leaf from flat, to a crinkly shape,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> like the shape of <a href="/wiki/Cabbage" title="Cabbage">cabbage</a> leaves. There also exist different <a href="/wiki/Morphogen" title="Morphogen">morphogen</a> gradients in a developing leaf which define the leaf's axis. Changes in these morphogen gradients may also affect the leaf form. Another very important class of regulators of leaf development are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MicroRNAs" title="MicroRNAs">microRNAs</a>, whose role in this process has just begun to be documented. The coming years should see a rapid development in comparative studies on leaf development, with many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expressed_Sequence_Tag" title="Expressed Sequence Tag">EST</a> sequences involved in the process coming online.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tree_form">Tree form</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Tree form">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Psaronius_double_section.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1368" data-file-width="1353" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Psaronius_double_section.JPG/220px-Psaronius_double_section.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Psaronius_double_section.JPG/330px-Psaronius_double_section.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Psaronius_double_section.JPG/440px-Psaronius_double_section.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Psaronius_double_section.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The trunk of early tree fern <i><a href="/wiki/Psaronius" title="Psaronius">Psaronius</a></i>, showing internal structure. The top of the plant would have been to the left of the image</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LepidodendronOhio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1251" data-file-width="1821" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/LepidodendronOhio.jpg/220px-LepidodendronOhio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/LepidodendronOhio.jpg/330px-LepidodendronOhio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/LepidodendronOhio.jpg/440px-LepidodendronOhio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LepidodendronOhio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
External mold of <i><a href="/wiki/Lepidodendron" title="Lepidodendron">Lepidodendron</a></i> trunk showing leaf scars from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Carboniferous" title="Upper Carboniferous">Upper Carboniferous</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The early Devonian landscape was devoid of vegetation taller than waist height. Without the evolution of a robust vascular system, taller heights could not be attained. There was, however, a constant evolutionary pressure to attain greater height. The most obvious advantage is the harvesting of more sunlight for photosynthesis – by overshadowing competitors – but a further advantage is present in spore distribution, as spores (and, later, seeds) can be blown greater distances if they start higher. This may be demonstrated by <i><a href="/wiki/Prototaxites" title="Prototaxites">Prototaxites</a></i>, thought to be a Palaeozoic fungus reaching eight metres in height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyce2007_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boyce2007-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>To attain <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/arborescence" title="wikt:arborescence">arborescence</a>, early plants had to develop <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">woody</a> tissue that provided support and water transport. The stele of plants undergoing "secondary growth" is surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Vascular_cambium" title="Vascular cambium">vascular cambium</a>, a ring of cells which produces more xylem (on the inside) and phloem (on the outside). Since xylem cells comprise dead, lignified tissue, subsequent rings of xylem are added to those already present, forming wood.</p>
<p>The first plants to develop this secondary growth, and a woody habit, were apparently the ferns, and as early as the Middle Devonian one species, <i><a href="/wiki/Wattieza" title="Wattieza">Wattieza</a></i>, had already reached heights of 8 m and a tree-like habit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein2007_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2007-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other clades did not take long to develop a tree-like stature; the Late Devonian <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeopteris" title="Archaeopteris">Archaeopteris</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Progymnosperm" title="Progymnosperm">precursor</a> to <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">gymnosperms</a> which evolved from the trimerophytes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retallack1985_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Retallack1985-71">[71]</a></sup> reached 30 m in height. These progymnosperms were the first plants to develop true wood, grown from a bifacial cambium, of which the first appearance is in the Middle Devonian <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rellimia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rellimia (page does not exist)">Rellimia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dannenhoffer1989_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dannenhoffer1989-72">[72]</a></sup> True wood is only thought to have evolved once, giving rise to the concept of a "lignophyte" clade.</p>
<p>These <i>Archaeopteris</i> forests were soon supplemented by lycopods, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Lepidodendrales" title="Lepidodendrales">lepidodendrales</a>, which topped 50m in height and 2m across at the base. These lycopods rose to dominate Late Devonian and Carboniferous coal deposits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis2004_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis2004-73">[73]</a></sup> Lepidodendrales differ from modern trees in exhibiting determinate growth: after building up a reserve of nutrients at a lower height, the plants would "bolt" to a genetically determined height, branch at that level, spread their spores and die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donoghue2005_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donoghue2005-74">[74]</a></sup> They consisted of "cheap" wood to allow their rapid growth, with at least half of their stems comprising a pith-filled cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup> Their wood was also generated by a unifacial vascular cambium – it did not produce new phloem, meaning that the trunks could not grow wider over time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (April 2008)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horsetail" title="Horsetail">horsetail</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Calamites" title="Calamites">Calamites</a></i> was next on the scene, appearing in the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a>. Unlike the modern horsetail <i><a href="/wiki/Equisetum" title="Equisetum">Equisetum</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Calamites" title="Calamites">Calamites</a></i> had a unifacial vascular cambium, allowing them to develop wood and grow to heights in excess of 10 m. They also branched multiple times.</p>
<p>While the form of early trees was similar to that of today's, the groups containing all modern trees had yet to evolve.</p>
<p>The dominant groups today are the gymnosperms, which include the coniferous trees, and the angiosperms, which contain all fruiting and flowering trees. It was long thought that the angiosperms arose from within the gymnosperms, but recent molecular evidence suggests that their living representatives form two distinct groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowe2000_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowe2000-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaw2000_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaw2000-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soltis2002_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soltis2002-77">[77]</a></sup> The molecular data has yet to be fully reconciled with morphological data,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Friis2006_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Friis2006-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton2006_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hilton2006-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bateman2006_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bateman2006-80">[80]</a></sup> but it is becoming accepted that the morphological support for paraphyly is not especially strong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frohlich2007_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frohlich2007-81">[81]</a></sup> This would lead to the conclusion that both groups arose from within the pteridosperms, probably as early as the <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frohlich2007_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frohlich2007-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The angiosperms and their ancestors played a very small role until they diversified during the Cretaceous. They started out as small, damp-loving organisms in the understory, and have been diversifying ever since the mid<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (April 2008)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>-Cretaceous, to become the dominant member of non-<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/boreal" title="wikt:boreal">boreal</a> forests today.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roots">Roots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Roots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table align="right" class="wikitable" style="margin:0 0 0 1em; text-align:center" width="300">
<tr>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lepidodendron_aculeatum2.jpg"><img alt="Lepidodendron aculeatum2.jpg" data-file-height="265" data-file-width="400" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Lepidodendron_aculeatum2.jpg/220px-Lepidodendron_aculeatum2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Lepidodendron_aculeatum2.jpg/330px-Lepidodendron_aculeatum2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Lepidodendron_aculeatum2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lepido_root_top.jpg"><img alt="Lepido root top.jpg" data-file-height="1575" data-file-width="2250" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Lepido_root_top.jpg/220px-Lepido_root_top.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Lepido_root_top.jpg/330px-Lepido_root_top.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Lepido_root_top.jpg/440px-Lepido_root_top.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>The roots (bottom image) of Lepidodendrales (Stigmaria) are thought to be functionally equivalent to the stems (top), as the similar appearance of "leaf scars" and "root scars" on these specimens from different species demonstrates.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Roots are important to plants for two main reasons: Firstly, they provide anchorage to the substrate; more importantly, <a href="/wiki/Absorption_of_water" title="Absorption of water">they provide a source of water</a> and nutrients from the soil. Roots allowed plants to grow taller and faster.</p>
<p>The onset of roots also had effects on a global scale. By disturbing the soil, and promoting its acidification (by taking up nutrients such as nitrate and phosphate<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (April 2008)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>), they enabled it to weather more deeply, promoting the draw-down of CO<sub>2</sub><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mora1996_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mora1996-82">[82]</a></sup> with huge implications for climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berner1994_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berner1994-83">[83]</a></sup> These effects may have been so profound they led to <a href="/wiki/Late_Devonian_extinction" title="Late Devonian extinction">a mass extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1995_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1995-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>But, how and when did roots evolve in the first place? While there are traces of root-like impressions in fossil soils in the Late Silurian,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retallack1986_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Retallack1986-85">[85]</a></sup> body fossils show the earliest plants to be devoid of roots. Many had tendrils that sprawled along or beneath the ground, with upright axes or <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/thalli" title="wikt:thalli">thalli</a> dotted here and there, and some even had non-photosynthetic subterranean branches which lacked stomata. The distinction between root and specialised branch is developmental; true roots follow a different developmental trajectory to stems. Further, roots differ in their branching pattern, and in possession of a <a href="/wiki/Root_cap" title="Root cap">root cap</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> So while Siluro-Devonian plants such as <i><a href="/wiki/Rhynia" title="Rhynia">Rhynia</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Horneophyton" title="Horneophyton">Horneophyton</a></i> possessed the physiological equivalent of roots,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> roots – defined as organs differentiated from stems – did not arrive until later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> Unfortunately, roots are rarely preserved in the fossil record, and our understanding of their evolutionary origin is sparse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rhizoids – small structures performing the same role as roots, usually a cell in diameter – probably evolved very early, perhaps even before plants colonised the land; they are recognised in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Characeae" title="Characeae">Characeae</a>, an algal sister group to land plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> That said, rhizoids probably evolved more than once; the rhizines of <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichens</a>, for example, perform a similar role. Even some animals (<i><a href="/wiki/Lamellibrachia" title="Lamellibrachia">Lamellibrachia</a></i>) have root-like structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>More advanced structures are common in the Rhynie chert, and many other fossils of comparable early Devonian age bear structures that look like, and acted like, roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-9"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> The rhyniophytes bore fine rhizoids, and the trimerophytes and herbaceous lycopods of the chert bore root-like structure penetrating a few centimetres into the soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1998_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1998-86">[86]</a></sup> However, none of these fossils display all the features borne by modern roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-10"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> Roots and root-like structures became increasingly more common and deeper penetrating during the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a>, with lycopod trees forming roots around 20 cm long during the Eifelian and Givetian. These were joined by progymnosperms, which rooted up to about a metre deep, during the ensuing Frasnian stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1998_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1998-86">[86]</a></sup> True gymnosperms and zygopterid ferns also formed shallow rooting systems during the Famennian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1998_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1998-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rhizophores of the lycopods provide a slightly different approach to rooting. They were equivalent to stems, with organs equivalent to leaves performing the role of rootlets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-11"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> A similar construction is observed in the extant lycopod <i>Isoetes</i>, and this appears to be evidence that roots evolved independently at least twice, in the lycophytes and other plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-12"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> a proposition supported by studies showing that roots are initiated and their growth promoted by different mechanisms in lycophytes and euphyllophytes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coudert2012_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coudert2012-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>A vascular system is indispensable to rooted plants, as non-photosynthesising roots need a supply of sugars, and a vascular system is required to transport water and nutrients from the roots to the rest of the plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KCbook_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-KCbook-12">[12]</a></sup> These plants are little more advanced than their Silurian forebears, without a dedicated root system; however, the flat-lying axes can be clearly seen to have growths similar to the rhizoids of bryophytes today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KCpaper_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-KCpaper-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the Middle to Late Devonian, most groups of plants had independently developed a rooting system of some nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KCpaper_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-KCpaper-88">[88]</a></sup> As roots became larger, they could support larger trees, and the soil was weathered to a greater depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1995_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1995-84">[84]</a></sup> This deeper weathering had effects not only on the aforementioned drawdown of CO<sub>2</sub>, but also opened up new habitats for colonisation by fungi and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1998_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1998-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Roots today have developed to the physical limits. They penetrate many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (April 2008)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> metres of soil to tap the water table.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (April 2008)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The narrowest roots are a mere 40 μm in diameter, and could not physically transport water if they were any narrower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-13"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup> The earliest fossil roots recovered, by contrast, narrowed from 3 mm to under 700 μm in diameter; of course, <a href="/wiki/Taphonomy" title="Taphonomy">taphonomy</a> is the ultimate control of what thickness can be seen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2001_10-14"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2001-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arbuscular_mycorrhizae">Arbuscular mycorrhizae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Arbuscular mycorrhizae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The efficiency of many plants' roots is increased via a <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/symbiotic" title="wikt:symbiotic">symbiotic</a> relationship with a fungal partner. The most common are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arbuscular_mycorrhizae" title="Arbuscular mycorrhizae">arbuscular mycorrhizae</a> (AM), literally "tree-like fungal roots". These comprise fungi that invade some root cells, filling the cell membrane with their <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hypha" title="wikt:hypha">hyphae</a>. They feed on the plant's sugars, but return nutrients generated or extracted from the soil (especially phosphate), to which the plant would otherwise have no access.</p>
<p>This symbiosis appears to have evolved early in plant history. AM are found in all plant groups, and 80% of extant vascular plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schuessler_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-schuessler-89">[89]</a></sup> suggesting an early ancestry; a "plant"-fungus symbiosis may even have been the step that enabled them to colonise the land,.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simon_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-simon-90">[90]</a></sup> Such fungi increase the productivity even of simple plants such as liverworts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Humphreys2010_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Humphreys2010-91">[91]</a></sup>Indeed, AM are abundant in the Rhynie chert;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-remy_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-remy-92">[92]</a></sup> the association occurred even before there were true roots to colonise, and some have suggested that roots evolved to provide a more comfortable habitat for mycorrhizal fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brundrett2002_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brundrett2002-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seeds">Seeds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Seeds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trigonocarpus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="429" data-file-width="552" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Trigonocarpus.jpg/220px-Trigonocarpus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Trigonocarpus.jpg/330px-Trigonocarpus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Trigonocarpus.jpg/440px-Trigonocarpus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trigonocarpus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The fossil seed <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Trigonocarpus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Trigonocarpus (page does not exist)">Trigonocarpus</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:77px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Runcaria_megasporangium_and_cupule_drawing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1262" data-file-width="458" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Runcaria_megasporangium_and_cupule_drawing.jpg/75px-Runcaria_megasporangium_and_cupule_drawing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Runcaria_megasporangium_and_cupule_drawing.jpg/113px-Runcaria_megasporangium_and_cupule_drawing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Runcaria_megasporangium_and_cupule_drawing.jpg/150px-Runcaria_megasporangium_and_cupule_drawing.jpg 2x" width="75"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Runcaria_megasporangium_and_cupule_drawing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The transitional fossil <i>Runcaria</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Early land plants reproduced in the fashion of ferns: spores germinated into small gametophytes, which produced eggs and/or sperm. These sperm would swim across moist soils to find the female organs (archegonia) on the same or another gametophyte, where they would fuse with an egg to produce an embryo, which would germinate into a sporophyte.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1998_86-4"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1998-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Heterosporic plants, as their name suggests, bear spores of two sizes – microspores and megaspores. These would germinate to form microgametophytes and megagametophytes, respectively. This system paved the way for ovules and seeds: taken to the extreme, the megasporangia could bear only a single megaspore tetrad, and to complete the transition to true ovules, three of the megaspores in the original tetrad could be aborted, leaving one megaspore per megasporangium.</p>
<p>The transition to ovules continued with this megaspore being "boxed in" to its sporangium while it germinates. Then, the megagametophyte is contained within a waterproof integument, which forms the bulk of the seed. The microgametophyte – a pollen grain which has germinated from a microspore – is employed for dispersal, only releasing its desiccation-prone sperm when it reaches a receptive megagametophyte.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lycopods and sphenopsids got a fair way down the path to the seed habit without ever crossing the threshold. Fossil lycopod megaspores reaching 1 cm in diameter, and surrounded by vegetative tissue, are known (Lepidocarpon, Achlamydocarpon);– these even germinate into a megagametophyte <i>in situ</i>. However, they fall short of being ovules, since the nucellus, an inner spore-covering layer, does not completely enclose the spore. A very small slit (micropyle) remains, meaning that the megasporangium is still exposed to the atmosphere. This has two consequences – firstly, it means it is not fully resistant to desiccation, and secondly, sperm do not have to "burrow" to access the archegonia of the megaspore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>A Middle <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">precursor</a> to seed plants from Belgium has been identified predating the earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seed_plants" title="Seed plants">seed plants</a> by about 20 million years. <i><a href="/wiki/Runcaria" title="Runcaria">Runcaria</a></i>, small and radially symmetrical, is an integumented <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megasporangium" title="Megasporangium">megasporangium</a> surrounded by a cupule. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megasporangium" title="Megasporangium">megasporangium</a> bears an unopened distal extension protruding above the multilobed <a href="/wiki/Integument" title="Integument">integument</a>. It is suspected that the extension was involved in anemophilous <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a>. <i>Runcaria</i> sheds new light on the sequence of character acquisition leading to the seed. <i>Runcaria</i> has all of the qualities of seed plants except for a solid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seed_coat" title="Seed coat">seed coat</a> and a system to guide the pollen to the ovule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first spermatophytes (literally: "seed plants") – that is, the first plants to bear true seeds – are called <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pteridosperm" title="Pteridosperm">pteridosperms</a></b>: literally, "seed ferns", so called because their foliage consisted of fern-like fronds, although they were not closely related to ferns. The oldest fossil evidence of seed plants is of Late Devonian age, and they appear to have evolved out of an earlier group known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Progymnosperms" title="Progymnosperms">progymnosperms</a>. These early seed plants ranged from trees to small, rambling shrubs; like most early progymnosperms, they were woody plants with fern-like foliage. They all bore ovules, but no cones, fruit or similar. While it is difficult to track the early evolution of seeds, the lineage of the seed ferns may be traced from the simple trimerophytes through homosporous <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aneurophytes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aneurophytes (page does not exist)">Aneurophytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-12"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>This seed model is shared by basically all gymnosperms (literally: "naked seeds"), most of which encase their seeds in a woody cone or fleshy aril (the <a href="/wiki/Taxus" title="Taxus">yew</a>, for example), but none of which fully enclose their seeds. The angiosperms ("vessel seeds") are the only group to fully enclose the seed, in a carpel.</p>
<p>Fully enclosed seeds opened up a new pathway for plants to follow: that of <a href="/wiki/Seed_dormancy" title="Seed dormancy">seed dormancy</a>. The embryo, completely isolated from the external atmosphere and hence protected from desiccation, could survive some years of drought before germinating. Gymnosperm seeds from the Late Carboniferous have been found to contain embryos, suggesting a lengthy gap between fertilisation and germination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mapes1989_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mapes1989-95">[95]</a></sup> This period is associated with the entry into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_earth" title="Greenhouse earth">greenhouse earth</a> period, with an associated increase in aridity. This suggests that dormancy arose as a response to drier climatic conditions, where it became advantageous to wait for a moist period before germinating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mapes1989_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mapes1989-95">[95]</a></sup> This evolutionary breakthrough appears to have opened a floodgate: previously inhospitable areas, such as dry mountain slopes, could now be tolerated, and were soon covered by trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mapes1989_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mapes1989-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Seeds offered further advantages to their bearers: they increased the success rate of fertilised gametophytes, and because a nutrient store could be "packaged" in with the embryo, the seeds could germinate rapidly in inhospitable environments, reaching a size where it could fend for itself more quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1998_86-5"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1998-86">[86]</a></sup> For example, without an endosperm, seedlings growing in arid environments would not have the reserves to grow roots deep enough to reach the water table before they expired from dehydration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1998_86-6"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1998-86">[86]</a></sup> Likewise, seeds germinating in a gloomy understory require an additional reserve of energy to quickly grow high enough to capture sufficient light for self-sustenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1998_86-7"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1998-86">[86]</a></sup> A combination of these advantages gave seed plants the ecological edge over the previously dominant genus <i>Archaeopteris</i>, thus increasing the biodiversity of early forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeo1998_86-8"><a href="#cite_note-Algeo1998-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite these advantages, it is common for fertilized ovules to fail to mature as seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Also during seed dormancy (often associated with unpredictable and stressful conditions) DNA damages accumulate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Thus DNA damage appears to be a basic problem for survival of seed plants, just as <a href="/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring)#DNA_damages_are_a_major_problem_for_life" title="DNA damage (naturally occurring)">DNA damages are a major problem for life in general</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> (Bernstein and Bernstein, 1991).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flowers">Flowers<span id="Flowers"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Flowers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For a more ecological discussion on the evolution of flowers, see <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">Flower</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crossotheca_nodule.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="634" data-file-width="1000" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Crossotheca_nodule.JPG/220px-Crossotheca_nodule.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Crossotheca_nodule.JPG/330px-Crossotheca_nodule.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Crossotheca_nodule.JPG/440px-Crossotheca_nodule.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crossotheca_nodule.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The pollen bearing organs of the early "flower" <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Crossotheca&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Crossotheca (page does not exist)">Crossotheca</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Flowers are modified leaves possessed only by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperm" title="Angiosperm">angiosperms</a>, which are relatively late to appear in the fossil record. The group originated and diversified during the Early Cretaceous and became ecologically significant thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feild2011_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feild2011-101">[101]</a></sup> Flower-like structures first appear in the <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> records some ~130 mya, in the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawton-Rauh_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lawton-Rauh-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Colorful and/or pungent structures surround the cones of plants such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cycads" title="Cycads">cycads</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnetales" title="Gnetales">Gnetales</a>, making a strict definition of the term "flower" elusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bateman2006_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bateman2006-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main function of a flower is <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a>, which, before the evolution of the flower and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperms" title="Angiosperms">angiosperms</a>, was the job of microsporophylls and megasporophylls. A flower can be considered a powerful evolutionary <a href="/wiki/Innovation" title="Innovation">innovation</a>, because its presence allowed the plant world to access new means and mechanisms for reproduction.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Syncarp_evolution.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="255" data-file-width="170" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Syncarp_evolution.svg/220px-Syncarp_evolution.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Syncarp_evolution.svg/330px-Syncarp_evolution.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Syncarp_evolution.svg/440px-Syncarp_evolution.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Syncarp_evolution.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The evolution of syncarps.<br/>
a: sporangia borne at tips of leaf<br/>
b: Leaf curls up to protect sporangia<br/>
c: leaf curls to form enclosed roll<br/>
d: grouping of three rolls into a syncarp</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The flowering plants have long been assumed to have evolved from within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnosperms" title="Gymnosperms">gymnosperms</a>; according to the traditional morphological view, they are closely allied to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnetales" title="Gnetales">Gnetales</a>. However, as noted above, recent molecular evidence is at odds with this hypothesis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaw2000_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chaw2000-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soltis2002_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Soltis2002-77">[77]</a></sup> and further suggests that Gnetales are more closely related to some gymnosperm groups than angiosperms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowe2000_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowe2000-75">[75]</a></sup> and that <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/extant" title="wikt:extant">extant</a> gymnosperms form a distinct clade to the angiosperms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowe2000_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bowe2000-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaw2000_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chaw2000-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soltis2002_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Soltis2002-77">[77]</a></sup> the two clades diverging some <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=300">300</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nam2003_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nam2003-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gnetophyta#Classification" title="Gnetophyta">Gnetophyta § Classification</a></div>
<p>The relationship of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_group" title="Stem group">stem groups</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperms" title="Angiosperms">angiosperms</a> is important in determining the evolution of flowers. stem groups provide an insight into the state of earlier "forks" on the path to the current state. Convergence increases the risk of misidentifying stem groups. Since the protection of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megagametophyte" title="Megagametophyte">megagametophyte</a> is evolutionarily desirable, probably many separate groups evolved protective encasements independently. In flowers, this protection takes the form of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpel" title="Carpel">carpel</a>, evolved from a leaf and recruited into a protective role, shielding the ovules. These ovules are further protected by a double-walled <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/integument" title="wikt:integument">integument</a>.</p>
<p>Penetration of these protective layers needs something more than a free-floating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microgametophyte" title="Microgametophyte">microgametophyte</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperms" title="Angiosperms">Angiosperms</a> have pollen grains comprising just three cells. One cell is responsible for drilling down through the integuments, and creating a conduit for the two sperm cells to flow down. The megagametophyte has just seven cells; of these, one fuses with a sperm cell, forming the nucleus of the egg itself, and another joins with the other sperm, and dedicates itself to forming a nutrient-rich <a href="/wiki/Endosperm" title="Endosperm">endosperm</a>. The other cells take auxiliary roles.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2008)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This process of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_fertilisation" title="Double fertilisation">double fertilisation</a>" is unique and common to all angiosperms.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bennettitales-cycadeoidaceae.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1422" data-file-width="1473" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bennettitales-cycadeoidaceae.jpg/220px-Bennettitales-cycadeoidaceae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bennettitales-cycadeoidaceae.jpg/330px-Bennettitales-cycadeoidaceae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bennettitales-cycadeoidaceae.jpg/440px-Bennettitales-cycadeoidaceae.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bennettitales-cycadeoidaceae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The inflorescences of the <a href="/wiki/Bennettitales" title="Bennettitales">Bennettitales</a> are strikingly similar to flowers</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the fossil record, there are three intriguing groups which bore flower-like structures. The first is the <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> pteridosperm <i><a href="/wiki/Glossopteris" title="Glossopteris">Glossopteris</a></i>, which already bore recurved leaves resembling carpels. The <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Caytonia" title="Caytonia">Caytonia</a></i> is more flower-like still, with enclosed ovules – but only a single integument. Further, details of their pollen and stamens set them apart from true flowering plants.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bennettitales" title="Bennettitales">Bennettitales</a> bore remarkably flower-like organs, protected by whorls of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bract" title="wikt:bract">bracts</a> which may have played a similar role to the petals and sepals of true flowers; however, these flower-like structures evolved independently, as the Bennettitales are more closely related to <a href="/wiki/Cycad" title="Cycad">cycads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ginkgo" title="Ginkgo">ginkgos</a> than to the angiosperms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crepet2000_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crepet2000-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, no true flowers are found in any groups save those extant today. Most morphological and molecular analyses place <i><a href="/wiki/Amborella" title="Amborella">Amborella</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Nymphaeales" title="Nymphaeales">nymphaeales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austrobaileya" title="Austrobaileya">Austrobaileyaceae</a> in a basal clade called "ANA". This clade appear to have diverged in the early Cretaceous, around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=130">130</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> – around the same time as the <a href="/wiki/Archaefructus" title="Archaefructus">earliest fossil angiosperm</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun2002_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sun2002-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Friis2003_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Friis2003-106">[106]</a></sup> and just after the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Clavatipollenites&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Clavatipollenites (page does not exist)">first angiosperm-like pollen</a>, 136 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frohlich2007_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-Frohlich2007-81">[81]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Magnoliids" title="Magnoliids">magnoliids</a> diverged soon after, and a rapid radiation had produced eudicots and monocots by <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=125">125</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frohlich2007_81-3"><a href="#cite_note-Frohlich2007-81">[81]</a></sup> By the end of the Cretaceous <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=66">66</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, over 50% of today's angiosperm orders had evolved, and the clade accounted for 70% of global species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wing1998_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wing1998-107">[107]</a></sup> It was around this time that flowering trees became dominant over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StewartRothwell_6-13"><a href="#cite_note-StewartRothwell-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:498</sup></p>
<p>The features of the basal "ANA" groups suggest that angiosperms originated in dark, damp, frequently disturbed areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feild2004_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feild2004-108">[108]</a></sup> It appears that the angiosperms remained constrained to such habitats throughout the Cretaceous – occupying the niche of small herbs early in the successional series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wing1998_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wing1998-107">[107]</a></sup> This may have restricted their initial significance, but given them the flexibility that accounted for the rapidity of their later diversifications in other habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feild2004_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Feild2004-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Phylogeny of anthophytes and gymnosperms, from <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crepet2000_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crepet2000-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
