<p>Some propose that the Angiosperms arose from an unknown Seed Fern, Pteridophyte, and view Cycads as living Seed Ferns with both Seed-Bearing and sterile leaves (Cycas revoluta)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton2006_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hilton2006-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_the_flower">Origins of the flower</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Origins of the flower">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amborella.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="223" data-file-width="250" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Amborella.jpg/200px-Amborella.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/fa/Amborella.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amborella.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A male flower of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amborella_trichopoda" title="Amborella trichopoda">Amborella trichopoda</a></i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amborellaceae" title="Amborellaceae">Amborellaceae</a> is considered the sister family of all other flowering plants.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amborellaceae" title="Amborellaceae">Amborellaceae</a> is regarded as being the sister <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">clade</a> to all other living flowering plants. The complete genome of <i>Amborella trichopoda</i> is still being sequenced as of March 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>. By comparing its genome with those of all other living flowering plants, it will be possible to work out the most likely characteristics of the ancestor of <i>A. trichopoda</i> and all other flowering plants, i.e. the ancestral flowering plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>It seems that on the level of the organ, the <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaf</a> may be the ancestor of the flower, or at least some floral organs. When some crucial genes involved in flower development are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutate" title="Mutate">mutated</a>, clusters of leaf-like structures arise in place of flowers. Thus, sometime in history, the developmental program leading to formation of a leaf must have been altered to generate a flower. There probably also exists an overall robust framework within which the floral diversity has been generated. An example of that is a gene called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LEAFY" title="LEAFY">LEAFY</a> (LFY)</i>, which is involved in flower development in <i><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis_thaliana" title="Arabidopsis thaliana">Arabidopsis thaliana</a></i>. The <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologs</a> of this gene are found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperms" title="Angiosperms">angiosperms</a> as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antirrhinum" title="Antirrhinum">snapdragon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">pea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> and even <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnosperms" title="Gymnosperms">gymnosperms</a>. Expression of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> LFY in distant plants like <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Citrus" title="Citrus">citrus</a> also results in flower-production in these plants. The <i>LFY</i> gene regulates the expression of some genes belonging to the <a href="/wiki/MADS-box" title="MADS-box">MADS-box</a> family. These genes, in turn, act as direct controllers of flower development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_the_MADS-box_family">Evolution of the MADS-box family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Evolution of the MADS-box family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The members of the <a href="/wiki/MADS-box" title="MADS-box">MADS-box</a> family of transcription factors play a very important and evolutionarily conserved role in flower development. According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_ABC_Model_of_Flower_Development" title="The ABC Model of Flower Development">ABC Model of flower development</a>, three zones — A,B and C — are generated within the developing flower primordium, by the action of some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_factors" title="Transcription factors">transcription factors</a>, that are members of the <a href="/wiki/MADS-box" title="MADS-box">MADS-box</a> family. Among these, the functions of the B and C domain genes have been evolutionarily more conserved than the A domain gene. Many of these genes have arisen through <a href="/wiki/Gene_duplication" title="Gene duplication">gene duplications</a> of ancestral members of this family. Quite a few of them show redundant functions.</p>
<p>The evolution of the <a href="/wiki/MADS-box" title="MADS-box">MADS-box</a> family has been extensively studied. These genes are present even in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pteridophytes" title="Pteridophytes">pteridophytes</a>, but the spread and diversity is many times higher in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperms" title="Angiosperms">angiosperms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> There appears to be quite a bit of pattern into how this family has evolved. Consider the evolution of the C-region gene <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AGAMOUS" title="AGAMOUS">AGAMOUS</a> (AG)</i>. It is expressed in today's flowers in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stamens" title="Stamens">stamens</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpel" title="Carpel">carpel</a>, which are reproductive organs. Its ancestor in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnosperms" title="Gymnosperms">gymnosperms</a> also has the same expression pattern. Here, it is expressed in the <a href="/wiki/Strobilus" title="Strobilus">strobili</a>, an organ that produces <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> or ovules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Similarly, the B-genes' <i>(AP3 and PI)</i> ancestors are expressed only in the male organs in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnosperms" title="Gymnosperms">gymnosperms</a>. Their descendants in the modern angiosperms also are expressed only in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stamens" title="Stamens">stamens</a>, the male reproductive organ. Thus, the same, then-existing components were used by the plants in a novel manner to generate the first flower. This is a recurring pattern in <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Factors_influencing_floral_diversity">Factors influencing floral diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Factors influencing floral diversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikiversity-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1000" height="32" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Wikiversity-logo.svg/40px-Wikiversity-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Wikiversity-logo.svg/60px-Wikiversity-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Wikiversity-logo.svg/80px-Wikiversity-logo.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikiversity" title="Wikiversity">Wikiversity</a> has bloom time data for <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikiversity.org/wiki/BCP/Linaria_vulgaris" title="v:BCP/Linaria vulgaris">Linaria vulgaris</a></b></i> on the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikiversity.org/wiki/Bloom_Clock" title="v:Bloom Clock">Bloom Clock</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>There is enormous variation in the developmental programs of plants. For example, grasses possess unique floral structures. The carpels and stamens are surrounded by scale-like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monocot" title="Monocot">lodicules</a> and two bracts: the lemma and the palea. Genetic evidence and morphology suggest that lodicules are homologous to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eudicot" title="Eudicot">eudicot</a> petals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompsom_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompsom-112">[112]</a></sup> The palea and lemma may be homologous to sepals in other groups, or may be unique grass structures. The genetic evidence is not clear.</p>
<p>Variation in floral structure is typically due to slight changes in the MADS-box genes and their expression pattern.</p>
<p>Another example is that of <i><a href="/wiki/Linaria_vulgaris" title="Linaria vulgaris">Linaria vulgaris</a></i>, which has two kinds of flower symmetries-<a href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_biology#Radial_symmetry" title="Symmetry in biology">radial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_biology#Bilateral_symmetry" title="Symmetry in biology">bilateral</a>. These symmetries are due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetic" title="Epigenetic">epigenetic</a> changes in just one gene called <i>CYCLOIDEA</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawton-Rauh_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lawton-Rauh-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pink_rose_albury_botanical_gardens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Pink_rose_albury_botanical_gardens.jpg/200px-Pink_rose_albury_botanical_gardens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Pink_rose_albury_botanical_gardens.jpg/300px-Pink_rose_albury_botanical_gardens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Pink_rose_albury_botanical_gardens.jpg/400px-Pink_rose_albury_botanical_gardens.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pink_rose_albury_botanical_gardens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Large number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petals" title="Petals">petals</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">roses</a> is the result of human selection</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> has a gene called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AGAMOUS" title="AGAMOUS">AGAMOUS</a></i> that plays an important role in defining how many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petals" title="Petals">petals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sepals" title="Sepals">sepals</a> and other organs are generated. Mutations in this gene give rise to the floral <a href="/wiki/Meristem" title="Meristem">meristem</a> obtaining an indeterminate fate, and many floral organs keep on getting produced. <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">Roses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnations" title="Carnations">carnations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morning_glory" title="Morning glory">morning glory</a>, for example, that have very dense floral organs. These flowers have been selected by horticulturists for increased number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petals" title="Petals">petals</a>. Researchers have found that the morphology of these flowers is because of strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutations" title="Mutations">mutations</a> in the <i>AGAMOUS</i> homolog in these plants, which leads to them making a large number of petals and sepals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Several studies on diverse plants like <a href="/wiki/Petunia" title="Petunia">petunia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Impatiens" title="Impatiens">Impatiens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> etc. have suggested that the enormous diversity of flowers is a result of small changes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genes" title="Genes">genes</a> controlling their development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of these changes also cause changes in expression patterns of the developmental genes, resulting in different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenotypes" title="Phenotypes">phenotypes</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Floral_Genome_Project" title="Floral Genome Project">Floral Genome Project</a> looked at the <a href="/wiki/Expressed_sequence_tag" title="Expressed sequence tag">EST</a> data from various tissues of many flowering plants. The researchers confirmed that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC_Model_of_flower_development" title="ABC Model of flower development">ABC Model of flower development</a> is not conserved across all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperms" title="Angiosperms">angiosperms</a>. Sometimes expression domains change, as in the case of many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monocots" title="Monocots">monocots</a>, and also in some basal angiosperms like <i><a href="/wiki/Amborella" title="Amborella">Amborella</a></i>. Different models of flower development like the <i>Fading boundaries model</i>, or the <i>Overlapping-boundaries model</i> which propose non-rigid domains of expression, may explain these architectures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> There is a possibility that from the basal to the modern angiosperms, the domains of floral architecture have gotten more and more fixed through evolution.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flowering_time">Flowering time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Flowering time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Another floral feature that has been a subject of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> is flowering time. Some plants flower early in their life cycle, others require a period of <a href="/wiki/Vernalization" title="Vernalization">vernalization</a> before flowering. This outcome is based on factors like <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intensity_(physics)" title="Intensity (physics)">light intensity</a>, presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pollinators" title="Pollinators">pollinators</a> and other environmental signals: genes like <i>CONSTANS (CO)</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Flowering_Locus_C" title="Flowering Locus C">Flowering Locus C</a></i> (<i>FLC</i>) and <i>FRIGIDA</i> regulate integration of environmental signals into the pathway for flower development. Variations in these loci have been associated with flowering time variations between plants. For example, <i><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis_thaliana" title="Arabidopsis thaliana">Arabidopsis thaliana</a></i> ecotypes that grow in the cold, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> regions require prolonged vernalization before they flower, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical" title="Tropical">tropical</a> varieties, and the most common lab strains, don't. This variation is due to mutations in the <i>FLC</i> and <i>FRIGIDA</i> genes, rendering them non-functional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Quite a few players in this process are conserved across all the plants studied. Sometimes though, despite genetic conservation, the mechanism of action turns out to be different. For example, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> is a short-day plant, while <i><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis_thaliana" title="Arabidopsis thaliana">Arabidopsis thaliana</a></i> is a long-day plant. Now, in both plants, the proteins <i>CO</i> and <i>FLOWERING LOCUS T (FT)</i> are present. But, in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>CO</i> enhances <i>FT</i> production, while in rice, the <i>CO</i> homolog represses <i>FT</i> production, resulting in completely opposite downstream effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_of_flower_evolution">Theories of flower evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Theories of flower evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Anthophyta" title="Anthophyta">Anthophyte theory</a> was based on the observation that a gymnospermic group <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnetae" title="Gnetae">Gnetales</a> has a flower-like <a href="/wiki/Ovule" title="Ovule">ovule</a>. It has partially developed <a href="/wiki/Vessel_element" title="Vessel element">vessels</a> as found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperms" title="Angiosperms">angiosperms</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megasporangium" title="Megasporangium">megasporangium</a> is covered by three envelopes, like the <a href="/wiki/Ovary" title="Ovary">ovary</a> structure of angiosperm flowers. However, many other lines of evidence show that Gnetales is not related to angiosperms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crepet2000_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-Crepet2000-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Mostly Male theory</i> has a more genetic basis. Proponents of this theory point out that the gymnosperms have two very similar copies of the gene <i>LFY</i>, while angiosperms just have one. <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">Molecular clock</a> analysis has shown that the other <i>LFY</i> paralog was lost in angiosperms around the same time as flower fossils become abundant, suggesting that this event might have led to floral evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> According to this theory, loss of one of the <i>LFY</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paralog" title="Paralog">paralog</a> led to flowers that were more male, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovules" title="Ovules">ovules</a> being expressed ectopically. These ovules initially performed the function of attracting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pollinators" title="Pollinators">pollinators</a>, but sometime later, may have been integrated into the core flower.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_photosynthetic_pathways">Evolution of photosynthetic pathways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Evolution of photosynthetic pathways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HatchSlackpathway2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="705" height="413" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/HatchSlackpathway2.svg/300px-HatchSlackpathway2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/HatchSlackpathway2.svg/450px-HatchSlackpathway2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/HatchSlackpathway2.svg/600px-HatchSlackpathway2.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HatchSlackpathway2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The C<sub>4</sub> carbon concentrating mechanism</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_photosynthesis" title="Evolution of photosynthesis">Evolution of photosynthesis</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C<sub>4</sub> metabolic pathway</a> is a valuable recent evolutionary innovation in plants, involving a complex set of adaptive changes to <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williamsjohnston_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-williamsjohnston-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">Photosynthesis</a> is not quite as simple as adding water to CO<sub>2</sub> to produce sugars and oxygen. A complex chemical pathway is involved, facilitated along the way by a range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzymes" title="Enzymes">enzymes</a> and co-enzymes. The <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> <a href="/wiki/RuBisCO" title="RuBisCO">RuBisCO</a> is responsible for "fixing" CO<sub>2</sub> – that is, it attaches it to a carbon-based molecule to form a sugar, which can be used by the plant, releasing an oxygen molecule along the way. However, the enzyme is notoriously inefficient, and just as effectively will also fix oxygen instead of CO<sub>2</sub> in a process called <a href="/wiki/Photorespiration" title="Photorespiration">photorespiration</a>. This is energetically costly as the plant has to use energy to turn the products of photorespiration back into a form that can react with CO<sub>2</sub>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concentrating_carbon">Concentrating carbon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Concentrating carbon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>C<sub>4</sub> plants evolved carbon concentrating mechanisms. These work by increasing the concentration of CO<sub>2</sub> around RuBisCO, thereby facilitating photosynthesis and decreasing photorespiration. The process of concentrating CO<sub>2</sub> around RuBisCO requires more energy than allowing gases to <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffuse</a>, but under certain conditions – i.e. warm temperatures (&gt;25 °C), low CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations, or high oxygen concentrations – pays off in terms of the decreased loss of sugars through photorespiration.</p>
<p>One type of C<sub>4</sub> metabolism employs a so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kranz_anatomy" title="Kranz anatomy">Kranz anatomy</a>. This transports CO<sub>2</sub> through an outer mesophyll layer, via a range of organic molecules, to the central bundle sheath cells, where the CO<sub>2</sub> is released. In this way, CO<sub>2</sub> is concentrated near the site of RuBisCO operation. Because RuBisCO is operating in an environment with much more CO<sub>2</sub> than it otherwise would be, it performs more efficiently.</p>
<p>A second mechanism, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CAM_photosynthesis" title="CAM photosynthesis">CAM photosynthesis</a>, temporally separates photosynthesis from the action of RuBisCO. RuBisCO only operates during the day, when stomata are sealed and CO<sub>2</sub> is provided by the breakdown of the chemical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malate" title="Malate">malate</a>. More CO<sub>2</sub> is then harvested from the atmosphere when stomata open, during the cool, moist nights, reducing water loss.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_record">Evolutionary record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Evolutionary record">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>These two pathways, with the same effect on RuBisCO, evolved a number of times independently – indeed, C<sub>4</sub> alone arose 62 times in 18 different plant <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a>. A number of 'pre-adaptations' seem to have paved the way for C4, leading to its clustering in certain clades: it has most frequently been innovated in plants that already had features such as extensive vascular bundle sheath tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christin2012_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christin2012-120">[120]</a></sup> Many potential evolutionary pathways resulting in the C<sub>4</sub> <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a> are possible and have been characterised using <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_inference" title="Bayesian inference">Bayesian inference</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williamsjohnston_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-williamsjohnston-119">[119]</a></sup> confirming that non-photosynthetic adaptations often provide evolutionary stepping stones for the further evolution of C<sub>4</sub>.</p>
<p>The C<sub>4</sub> construction is most famously used by a subset of grasses, while CAM is employed by many succulents and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cacti" title="Cacti">cacti</a>. The trait appears to have emerged during the <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a>, around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=25–32">25 to 32</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Osborne2006_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne2006-121">[121]</a></sup> however, they did not become ecologically significant until the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>, <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=6–7">6 to 7</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retallack1997_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Retallack1997-122">[122]</a></sup> Remarkably, some charcoalified fossils preserve tissue organised into the Kranz anatomy, with intact bundle sheath cells,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomasson1986_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomasson1986-123">[123]</a></sup> allowing the presence C<sub>4</sub> metabolism to be identified without doubt at this time. Isotopic markers are used to deduce their distribution and significance. C<sub>3</sub> plants preferentially use the lighter of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotopes" title="Isotopes">isotopes</a> of carbon in the atmosphere, <sup>12</sup>C, which is more readily involved in the chemical pathways involved in its fixation. Because C<sub>4</sub> metabolism involves a further chemical step, this effect is accentuated. Plant material can be <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">analysed</a> to deduce the ratio of the heavier <sup>13</sup>C to <sup>12</sup>C. This ratio is denoted <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C. C<sub>3</sub> plants are on average around 14‰ (parts per thousand) lighter than the atmospheric ratio, while C<sub>4</sub> plants are about 28‰ lighter. The <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C of CAM plants depends on the percentage of carbon fixed at night relative to what is fixed in the day, being closer to C<sub>3</sub> plants if they fix most carbon in the day and closer to C<sub>4</sub> plants if they fix all their carbon at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isotope_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-isotope-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>It's troublesome procuring original fossil material in sufficient quantity to analyse the grass itself, but fortunately there is a good proxy: horses. Horses were globally widespread in the period of interest, and browsed almost exclusively on grasses. There's an old phrase in isotope palæontology, "you are what you eat (plus a little bit)" – this refers to the fact that organisms reflect the isotopic composition of whatever they eat, plus a small adjustment factor. There is a good record of horse teeth throughout the globe, and their <span class="texhtml">δ</span><sup>13</sup>C has been measured. The record shows a sharp negative inflection around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=6–7">6 to 7</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, during the Messinian, and this is interpreted as the rise of C<sub>4</sub> plants on a global scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retallack1997_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Retallack1997-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="When_is_C4_an_advantage.3F">When is C<sub>4</sub> an advantage?</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: When is C4 an advantage?">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While C<sub>4</sub> enhances the efficiency of RuBisCO, the concentration of carbon is highly energy intensive. This means that C<sub>4</sub> plants only have an advantage over C<sub>3</sub> organisms in certain conditions: namely, high temperatures and low rainfall. C<sub>4</sub> plants also need high levels of sunlight to thrive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Osborne2009_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne2009-125">[125]</a></sup> Models suggest that, without wildfires removing shade-casting trees and shrubs, there would be no space for C<sub>4</sub> plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bond2005_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bond2005-126">[126]</a></sup> But, wildfires have occurred for 400 million years – why did C<sub>4</sub> take so long to arise, and then appear independently so many times? The Carboniferous (~<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=300">300</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>) had notoriously high oxygen levels – almost enough to allow <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_combustion" title="Spontaneous combustion">spontaneous combustion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> – and very low CO<sub>2</sub>, but there is no C<sub>4</sub> isotopic signature to be found. And there doesn't seem to be a sudden trigger for the Miocene rise.</p>
<p>During the Miocene, the atmosphere and climate were relatively stable. If anything, CO<sub>2</sub> increased gradually from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=14–9">14 to 9</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> before settling down to concentrations similar to the Holocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pagani2005_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pagani2005-128">[128]</a></sup> This suggests that it did not have a key role in invoking C<sub>4</sub> evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Osborne2006_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne2006-121">[121]</a></sup> Grasses themselves (the group which would give rise to the most occurrences of C<sub>4</sub>) had probably been around for 60 million years or more, so had had plenty of time to evolve C<sub>4</sub>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piperno2005_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piperno2005-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prasad2005_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prasad2005-130">[130]</a></sup> which, in any case, is present in a diverse range of groups and thus evolved independently. There is a strong signal of climate change in South Asia;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Osborne2006_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne2006-121">[121]</a></sup> increasing aridity – hence increasing fire frequency and intensity – may have led to an increase in the importance of grasslands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keeley2005_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keeley2005-131">[131]</a></sup> However, this is difficult to reconcile with the North American record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Osborne2006_121-3"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne2006-121">[121]</a></sup> It is possible that the signal is entirely biological, forced by the fire- (and elephant?)-<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retallack2001_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Retallack2001-132">[132]</a></sup> driven acceleration of grass evolution – which, both by increasing weathering and incorporating more carbon into sediments, reduced atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retallack2001_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Retallack2001-132">[132]</a></sup> Finally, there is evidence that the onset of C<sub>4</sub> from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=9–7">9 to 7</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> is a biased signal, which only holds true for North America, from where most samples originate; emerging evidence suggests that grasslands evolved to a dominant state at least 15Ma earlier in South America.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_transcriptional_regulation">Evolution of transcriptional regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Evolution of transcriptional regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Transcription factors and transcriptional regulatory networks play key roles in plant development and stress responses, as well as their evolution. During plant landing, many novel transcription factor families emerged and are preferentially wired into the networks of multicellular development, reproduction, and organ development, contributing to more complex morphogenesis of land plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBE_1767_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-MBE_1767-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_secondary_metabolism">Evolution of secondary metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Evolution of secondary metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Azadirachtin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="731" data-file-width="789" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Azadirachtin.png/250px-Azadirachtin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Azadirachtin.png/375px-Azadirachtin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Azadirachtin.png/500px-Azadirachtin.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Azadirachtin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Structure of <a href="/wiki/Azadirachtin" title="Azadirachtin">Azadirachtin</a>, a terpenoid produced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neem" title="Neem">Neem</a> plant, which helps ward off microbes and insects. Many secondary metabolites have complex structures</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_metabolites" title="Secondary metabolites">Secondary metabolites</a> are essentially low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_weight" title="Molecular weight">molecular weight</a> compounds, sometimes having complex structures. They function in processes as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a>, anti-herbivory, <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinator</a> attraction, <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a> between plants, maintaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbiotic" title="Symbiotic">symbiotic</a> associations with soil flora, enhancing the rate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization" title="Fertilization">fertilization</a> etc., and hence are significant from the evo-devo perspective. The structural and functional diversity of these secondary metabolites across the plant kingdom is vast; it is estimated that hundreds of thousands of enzymes might be involved in this process in the entire of the plant kingdom, with about 15–25% of the genome coding for these enzymes, and every species having its unique arsenal of secondary metabolites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Many of these metabolites are of enormous medical significance to humans.</p>
<p>What is the purpose of having so many secondary metabolites being produced, with a significant chunk of the <a href="/wiki/Metabolome" title="Metabolome">metabolome</a> devoted to this activity? It is hypothesized that most of these chemicals help in generating immunity and, in consequence, the diversity of these metabolites is a result of a constant war between plants and their parasites. There is evidence that this may be true in many cases. The big question here is the reproductive cost involved in maintaining such an impressive inventory. Various models have been suggested that probe into this aspect of the question, but a consensus on the extent of the cost is lacking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> We still cannot predict whether a plant with more secondary metabolites would be better off than other plants in its vicinity.</p>
<p>Secondary metabolite production seems to have arisen quite early during evolution. In plants, they seem to have spread out using mechanisms including gene duplications, evolution of novel genes etc. Furthermore, studies have shown that diversity in some of these compounds may be positively selected for.</p>
<p>Although the role of novel gene evolution in the evolution of secondary metabolism cannot be denied, there are several examples where new metabolites have been formed by small changes in the reaction. For example, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cyanogen_glycosides&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cyanogen glycosides (page does not exist)">cyanogen glycosides</a> have been proposed to have evolved multiple times in different plant lineages. There are several such instances of <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>. For example, enzymes for synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Limonene" title="Limonene">limonene</a> – a <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpene</a> – are more similar between angiosperms and gymnosperms than to their own terpene synthesis enzymes. This suggests independent evolution of the limonene biosynthetic pathway in these two lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms_and_players_in_evolution_of_plant_form">Mechanisms and players in evolution of plant form</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Mechanisms and players in evolution of plant form">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Microrna_secondary_structure.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="429" data-file-width="684" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Microrna_secondary_structure.png/220px-Microrna_secondary_structure.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Microrna_secondary_structure.png/330px-Microrna_secondary_structure.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Microrna_secondary_structure.png/440px-Microrna_secondary_structure.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Microrna_secondary_structure.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Stem-loop" title="Stem-loop">stem-loop</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_structure" title="Secondary structure">secondary structure</a> of a pre-microRNA from <i><a href="/wiki/Brassica_oleracea" title="Brassica oleracea">Brassica oleracea</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While environmental factors are significantly responsible for evolutionary change, they act merely as agents for <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>. Change is inherently brought about via phenomena at the genetic level - <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutations" title="Mutations">mutations</a>, chromosomal rearrangements and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetic" title="Epigenetic">epigenetic</a> changes. While the general types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutations" title="Mutations">mutations</a> hold true across the living world, in plants, some other mechanisms have been implicated as highly significant.</p>
<p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Genome_doubling&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Genome doubling (page does not exist)">Genome doubling</a> is a relatively common occurrence in plant evolution and results in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyploidy" title="Polyploidy">polyploidy</a>, which is consequently a common feature in plants. It is believed that at least half (and probably all) plants have seen genome doubling in their history. Genome doubling entails <a href="/wiki/Gene_duplication" title="Gene duplication">gene duplication</a>, thus generating functional redundancy in most genes. The duplicated genes may attain new function, either by changes in expression pattern or changes in activity. Polyploidy and gene duplication are believed to be among the most powerful forces in evolution of plant form; though it is not known why <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> doubling is such a frequent process in plants. One probable reason is the production of large amounts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_metabolites" title="Secondary metabolites">secondary metabolites</a> in plant cells. Some of them might interfere in the normal process of chromosomal segregation, causing genome duplication.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maize-teosinte.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="1037" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/150px-Maize-teosinte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/225px-Maize-teosinte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/300px-Maize-teosinte.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maize-teosinte.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Top: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a>, bottom: <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, middle: maize-teosinte hybrid</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In recent times, plants have been shown to possess significant <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">microRNA</a> families, which are conserved across many plant lineages. In comparison to <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, while the number of plant miRNA families are lesser than animals, the size of each family is much larger. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MiRNA" title="MiRNA">miRNA</a> genes are also much more spread out in the genome than those in animals, where they are more clustered. It has been proposed that these miRNA families have expanded by duplications of chromosomal regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Many miRNA genes involved in regulation of <a href="/wiki/Plant_development" title="Plant development">plant development</a> have been found to be quite conserved between plants studied.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">Domestication</a> of plants like <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> etc. has also been a significant driving force in their evolution. Some studies have tried to look at the origins of the <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> plant and it turns out that maize is a domesticated derivative of a wild plant from <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> called teosinte. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">Teosinte</a> belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i>Zea</i>, just as maize, but bears very small <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a>, 5-10 hard cobs and a highly branched and spread out stem.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cauliflower.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="189" data-file-width="172" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Cauliflower.JPG/150px-Cauliflower.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/25/Cauliflower.JPG 1.5x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cauliflower.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cauliflower" title="Cauliflower">Cauliflower</a> – <i>Brassica oleracea</i> var. <i>botrytis</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Interestingly, crosses between a particular teosinte variety and maize yields fertile offspring that are intermediate in <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a> between maize and teosinte. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/QTL" title="QTL">QTL</a> analysis has also revealed some loci that, when mutated in maize, yield a teosinte-like stem or teosinte-like cobs. <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">Molecular clock</a> analysis of these genes estimates their origins to some 9,000 years ago, well in accordance with other records of maize domestication. It is believed that a small group of farmers must have selected some maize-like natural mutant of teosinte some 9,000 years ago in Mexico, and subjected it to continuous selection to yield the familiar maize plant of today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another interesting case is that of <a href="/wiki/Cauliflower" title="Cauliflower">cauliflower</a>. The edible cauliflower is a domesticated version of the wild plant <i><a href="/wiki/Brassica_oleracea" title="Brassica oleracea">Brassica oleracea</a></i>, which does not possess the dense undifferentiated <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a>, called the curd, that cauliflower possesses.</p>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikispecies-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1103" data-file-width="941" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikispecies-logo.svg/34px-Wikispecies-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikispecies-logo.svg/51px-Wikispecies-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikispecies-logo.svg/68px-Wikispecies-logo.svg.png 2x" width="34"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikispecies" title="Wikispecies">Wikispecies</a> has information related to: <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://species.wikimedia.org/wiki/Brassicaceae" title="wikispecies:Brassicaceae">Brassicaceae</a></b></i></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Cauliflower possesses a single mutation in a gene called <i>CAL</i>, controlling <a href="/wiki/Meristem" title="Meristem">meristem</a> differentiation into <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a>. This causes the cells at the floral meristem to gain an undifferentiated identity and, instead of growing into a <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flower</a>, they grow into a lump of undifferentiated cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> This mutation has been selected through domestication since at least the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> empire.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coevolution_of_Plants_and_Fungal_Parasites">Coevolution of Plants and Fungal Parasites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evolutionary_history_of_plants&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Coevolution of Plants and Fungal Parasites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>An additional contributing factor in some plants leading to evolutionary change is the force due to coevolution with fungal parasites. In an environment with a fungal parasite, which is common in nature, the plants must make adaptation in an attempt to evade the harmful effects of the parasite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capelle2005_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Capelle2005-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Whenever a parasitic fungus is siphoning limited resources away from a plant, there is selective pressure for a phenotype that is better able to prevent parasitic attack from fungi. At the same time, fungi that are better equipped to evade the defenses of the plant will have greater fitness level. The combination of these two factors leads to an endless cycle of evolutionary change in the host-pathogen system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because each species in the relationship is influenced by a constantly changing symbiont, evolutionary change usually occurs at a faster pace than if the other species was not present. This is true of most instances of coevolution. This makes the ability of a population to quickly evolve vital to its survival. Also, if the pathogenic species is too successful and threatens the survival and reproductive success of the host plants, the pathogenic fungi risk losing their nutrient source for future generations. These factors create a dynamic that shapes the evolutionary changes in both species generation after generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Genes that code for defense mechanisms in plants must keep changing to keep up with the parasite that constantly works to evade the defenses. Genes that code for attachment mechanisms are the most dynamic and are directly related to the evading ability of the fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The greater the changes in these genes, the more change in the attachment mechanism. After selective forces on the resulting phenotypes, evolutionary change that promotes evasion of host defenses occurs.</p>
<p>Fungi not only evolve to avoid the defenses of the plants, but they also attempt to prevent the plant from enacting the mechanisms to improve its defenses. Anything the fungi can do to slow the evolution process of the host plants will improve the fitness of future generations because the plant will not be able to keep up with the evolutionary changes of the parasite. One of the main processes by which plants quickly evolve in response to the environment is sexual reproduction. Without sexual reproduction, advantageous traits could not be spread through the plant population as quickly allowing the fungi to gain a competitive advantage. For this reason, the sexual reproductive organs of plants are targets for attacks by fungi. Studies have shown that many different current types of obligate parasitic plant fungi have developed mechanisms to disable or otherwise affect the sexual reproduction of the plants. If successful, the sexual reproduction process slows for the plant, thus slowing down evolutionary change or in extreme cases, the fungi can render the plant sterile creating an advantage for the pathogens. It is unknown exactly how this adaptive trait developed in fungi, but it is clear that the relationship to the plant forced the development of the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some researchers are also studying how a range of factors affect the rate of evolutionary change and the outcomes of change in different environments. For example, as with most evolution, increases in heritability in a population allow for a greater evolutionary response in the presence of selective pressure. For traits specific to the plant-fungi coevolution, researchers have studied how the virulence of the invading pathogen affects the coevolution. Studies involving Mycosphaerella graminicola have consistently showed that virulence of a pathogen does not have a significant impact on the evolutionary track of the host plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>There can be other factors in that can affect the process of coevolution. For example, in small populations, selection is a relatively weaker force on the population due to genetic drift. Genetic drift increases the likelihood of having fixed alleles which decreases the genetic variance in the population. Therefore, if there is only a small population of plants in an area with the ability to reproduce together, genetic drift may counteract the effects of selection putting the plant in a disadvantageous position to fungi which can evolve at a normal rate. The variance in both the host and pathogen population is a major determinant of evolutionary success compared to the other species. The greater the genetic variance, the faster the species can evolve to counteract the other organism’s avoidance or defensive mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capelle2005_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Capelle2005-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to the process of pollination for plants, the effective population size is normally larger than for fungi because pollinators can link isolated populations in a way that the fungus is not able. This means positive traits that evolve in non-adjacent but close areas can be passed to nearby areas. Fungi must individually evolve to evade host defenses in each area. This is obviously a clear competitive advantage for the host plants. Sexual reproduction with a broad, high variance population leads to fast evolutionary change and higher reproductive success of offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Environment and climate patterns also play a role in evolutionary outcomes. Studies with oak trees and an obligate fungal parasite at different altitudes clearly show this distinction. For the same species, different altitudinal positions had drastically different rates of evolution and changes in the response to the pathogens due to the organism also in a selective environment due to their surroundings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coevolution is a process that is related to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_queen_hypothesis" title="Red queen hypothesis">red queen hypothesis</a>. Both the host plant and parasitic fungi have to continue to survive to stay in their ecological niche. If one of the two species in the relationship evolves at a significantly faster rate than the other, the slower species will be at a competitive disadvantage and risk the loss of nutrients. Because the two species in the system are so closely linked, they respond to external environment factors together and each species affects the evolutionary outcome of the other. In other words, each species exerts selective pressure on the other. Population size is also a major factor in the outcome because differences in gene flow and genetic drift could cause evolutionary changes that do not match the direction of selection expected by forces due to the other organism. Coevolution is an important phenomenon necessary for understanding the vital relationship between plants and their fungal parasites.</p>
